Elemental Bodies: Eternal Aspects
Explanation of the eternal aspects of elemental bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which aspects of elemental bodies are considered eternal in the Aristotelian framework.

Core Idea
Elemental bodies are eternal in matter, corporeal form by type, and specific forms by genus.

The author now turns to elemental (earthly) bodies. He explains that they are eternal in three respects: their matter, their corporeal form (as a type that persists through individuals), and their specific forms (as a genus that persists through various species). This lesson focuses on these eternal dimensions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the elemental bodies passage
Read from 'وأما العنصريات' up to 'بتعاقب أنواعه'.
This covers the eternal aspects of elemental bodies.
2
Identify the three eternal aspects
The passage lists matter, corporeal form by type, and specific forms by genus. Note the distinct ways each is eternal.
Corporeal form is a single type; specific forms share a genus.
3
Understand the reasoning
For corporeal form: matter is never devoid of it, and its type persists through individuals. For specific forms: matter must always have one, and their genus persists through types.
The continuity is through succession, not individual permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنها قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها وإليه ذهب أرسطو ومن تبعه من متأخري الفلاسفة كالفارابي وابن سينا، وتفصيل مذهبهم أنهم قالوا : الأجسام تنقسم كما علمت إلى فلكيات وعنصريات، أما الفلكيات فإنها قديمة بموادها وصورها الجسمية والنوعية ( وأعراضها ( المعينة من المقادير والأشكال وغيرها ) إلا الحركات والأوضاع المشخصة فإنها حادثة ) قطعاً ضرورة أن كل حركة شخصية مسبوقة بأخرى لا إلى نهاية وكذا الأوضاع المعينة التابعة لها، وأما مطلق الحركة والوضع فقديم أيضاً لأن مذهبهم أن الأفلاك متحركة مستمرة من الأزل إلى الأبد بلا سكون أصلاً . ( وأما العنصريات فقديمة بموادها وبصورها الجسمية (بنوعها وذلك لأن المادة لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية التي هي طبيعة واحدة نوعية لا تختلف إلا بأمور خارجة عن حقيقتها فيكون نوعها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أفرادها أزلاً وأبداً ، ) وبصورها النوعية بجنسها ) وذلك لأن مادتها لا يجوز خلوها عن صورها النوعية بأسرها بل لا بد أن يكون معها واحدة منها لكن هذه الصور متشاركة في جنسها دون ماهيتها النوعية فيكون جنسها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أنواعه (نعم) الصور المشخصة فيهما أي في الصورة الجسمية والنوعية ( والأعراض المختصة ( المعينة ( محدثة ولا امتناع في حدوث بعض الصور النوعية ) العنصرية كأن يكون مثلاً نوع الإنسان حادثاً غير مستمر الوجود تتعاقب أفراده الشخصية، إذ يجوز حصوله من عنصر آخر بطريق الكون والفساد ولا امتناع أيضاً عندهم في استمراره كذلك ولا في استمرار أنواع المركبات في ضمن أفرادها المتعاقبة بلا نهاية
The second: They are eternal in their essences and attributes, and to it went Aristotle and those who followed him among the later philosophers, like al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. The detail of their doctrine is that they said: Bodies are divided, as you have learned, into celestial and elemental. As for celestial bodies, they are eternal in their matters, their corporeal and specific forms, and their determinate accidents – quantities, shapes, and others – except for the individual motions and positions, for they are originated, necessarily, because every individual motion is preceded by another ad infinitum, and likewise the determinate positions dependent on them. As for absolute motion and position, they are also eternal, because their doctrine is that the celestial spheres are in continuous motion from eternity to eternity without any rest at all. As for elemental bodies, they are eternal in their matters and in their corporeal forms by their type, because matter is never devoid of the corporeal form, which is a single nature of a type that does not differ except by matters external to its reality; so its type is continuously existent through the succession of its individuals eternally and forever; and in their specific forms by their genus, because their matter cannot be devoid of all their specific forms, but rather must have one of them with it. However, these forms share in their genus but not in their specific quiddity; so their genus is continuously existent through the succession of its types. Indeed, the individualizing forms in both – that is, in the corporeal and specific forms – and the specific determinate accidents are originated. There is no impossibility in the origination of some elemental specific forms, such as for example the human type being originated, not continuously existent, with its personal individuals succeeding one another, for it is possible that it arises from another element by way of generation and corruption. And there is also no impossibility in their view in its continuation thus, nor in the continuation of the types of compounds within their successive individuals ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the second division of bodies – that they are eternal in both essence and attributes. The author attributes this view to Aristotle and his later followers, al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. The exposition then provides a detailed breakdown of the philosophers' position by dividing bodies into celestial (falakiyyāt) and elemental (ʿunṣuriyyāt). For celestial bodies, they are eternal in matter, corporeal form, specific form, and determinate accidents (quantities, shapes, etc.) – except for individual motions (ḥarakāt shakhṣiyya) and positions (awḍāʿ), which are originated. The reason given is that each individual motion is preceded by another to infinity, so there cannot be a first motion. However, motion in the absolute sense (muṭlaq al-ḥaraka) and position (al-waḍʿ) are eternal because the celestial spheres move continuously from pre‑eternity to post‑eternity without ever ceasing. For elemental bodies, the philosophers hold them eternal in matter and in corporeal form by type (bi‑nawʿihā), because matter always has a corporeal form of a single type, and the type persists through successive individuals. They are also eternal in specific forms by genus (bi‑jinsihā), because matter must always have some specific form, and these specific forms share a genus that persists through successive types. However, the individualizing forms (al-ṣuwar al-mushakhkhiṣa) in both corporeal and specific forms, as well as the determinate accidents, are originated. The author adds that there is no contradiction in the view that some elemental specific forms (like the human type) may be originated and not continuously existent, as they could arise from another element via generation and corruption. Similarly, the continuation of such types or the types of compounds within successive individuals ad infinitum is possible in their doctrine. This detailed exposition clarifies the nuanced stance of the Peripatetic philosophers.

Key Terms
عنصريات
صورة جسمية
صورة نوعية
جنس
نوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that elemental bodies are eternal in all aspects, including individual forms.
Correction: Only matter, corporeal form by type, and specific forms by genus are eternal; individual forms and accidents are originated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the corporeal form of elemental bodies eternal?
A) Each individual corporeal form is eternal.
B) The type of corporeal form persists through successive individuals.
C) The genus of corporeal form persists through successive types.
D) Corporeal form is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Elemental bodies are eternal in matter, in the type of corporeal form, and in the genus of specific forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your physical body is just a temporary individual in a long chain of the same type, how should that change the way you care for your body and how you view its eventual decay? What does this say about your personal identity beyond the physical?
?
The passage says matter always has some specific form; you never encounter formless matter. Does that make you more aware of the order and purpose in every material thing? How can you train yourself to see the Creator's design in the ordinary objects around you?
?
If the genus of specific forms persists eternally, then every material thing has a necessary continuity. Does that comfort you or unnerve you? How does this affect your belief in the Hereafter, where the material world will be replaced? Will you live today as if this world's forms are temporary or eternal?

Elemental Bodies: Originated Aspects and Possibilities
Explanation of originated aspects and the possibility of originated specific types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which aspects of elemental bodies are originated and the possibility of originated specific types like humans.

Core Idea
Individual forms and accidents are originated, and even some specific types may be originated.

After discussing what is eternal in elemental bodies, the author turns to what is originated. He states that individualizing forms and determinate accidents are originated. Moreover, he notes that it is possible for some specific forms (like the human type) to be originated and to persist through successive individuals, a view compatible with the Aristotelian framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the final section
Read from 'نعم الصور المشخصة' to the end of the passage.
This covers originated aspects and the possibility of originated types.
2
Identify originated aspects
The passage mentions individualizing forms in both corporeal and specific forms, as well as determinate accidents, as originated.
These are the changing particulars.
3
Understand the possibility of originated types
The author says there is no impossibility in some specific forms (e.g., human) being originated and then continuing through individuals.
This shows flexibility in the doctrine.
4
Note the implication
The passage concludes that the continuation of types and compounds ad infinitum is also possible.
This aligns with the idea of eternal succession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنها قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها وإليه ذهب أرسطو ومن تبعه من متأخري الفلاسفة كالفارابي وابن سينا، وتفصيل مذهبهم أنهم قالوا : الأجسام تنقسم كما علمت إلى فلكيات وعنصريات، أما الفلكيات فإنها قديمة بموادها وصورها الجسمية والنوعية ( وأعراضها ( المعينة من المقادير والأشكال وغيرها ) إلا الحركات والأوضاع المشخصة فإنها حادثة ) قطعاً ضرورة أن كل حركة شخصية مسبوقة بأخرى لا إلى نهاية وكذا الأوضاع المعينة التابعة لها، وأما مطلق الحركة والوضع فقديم أيضاً لأن مذهبهم أن الأفلاك متحركة مستمرة من الأزل إلى الأبد بلا سكون أصلاً . ( وأما العنصريات فقديمة بموادها وبصورها الجسمية (بنوعها وذلك لأن المادة لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية التي هي طبيعة واحدة نوعية لا تختلف إلا بأمور خارجة عن حقيقتها فيكون نوعها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أفرادها أزلاً وأبداً ، ) وبصورها النوعية بجنسها ) وذلك لأن مادتها لا يجوز خلوها عن صورها النوعية بأسرها بل لا بد أن يكون معها واحدة منها لكن هذه الصور متشاركة في جنسها دون ماهيتها النوعية فيكون جنسها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أنواعه (نعم) الصور المشخصة فيهما أي في الصورة الجسمية والنوعية ( والأعراض المختصة ( المعينة ( محدثة ولا امتناع في حدوث بعض الصور النوعية ) العنصرية كأن يكون مثلاً نوع الإنسان حادثاً غير مستمر الوجود تتعاقب أفراده الشخصية، إذ يجوز حصوله من عنصر آخر بطريق الكون والفساد ولا امتناع أيضاً عندهم في استمراره كذلك ولا في استمرار أنواع المركبات في ضمن أفرادها المتعاقبة بلا نهاية
The second: They are eternal in their essences and attributes, and to it went Aristotle and those who followed him among the later philosophers, like al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. The detail of their doctrine is that they said: Bodies are divided, as you have learned, into celestial and elemental. As for celestial bodies, they are eternal in their matters, their corporeal and specific forms, and their determinate accidents – quantities, shapes, and others – except for the individual motions and positions, for they are originated, necessarily, because every individual motion is preceded by another ad infinitum, and likewise the determinate positions dependent on them. As for absolute motion and position, they are also eternal, because their doctrine is that the celestial spheres are in continuous motion from eternity to eternity without any rest at all. As for elemental bodies, they are eternal in their matters and in their corporeal forms by their type, because matter is never devoid of the corporeal form, which is a single nature of a type that does not differ except by matters external to its reality; so its type is continuously existent through the succession of its individuals eternally and forever; and in their specific forms by their genus, because their matter cannot be devoid of all their specific forms, but rather must have one of them with it. However, these forms share in their genus but not in their specific quiddity; so their genus is continuously existent through the succession of its types. Indeed, the individualizing forms in both – that is, in the corporeal and specific forms – and the specific determinate accidents are originated. There is no impossibility in the origination of some elemental specific forms, such as for example the human type being originated, not continuously existent, with its personal individuals succeeding one another, for it is possible that it arises from another element by way of generation and corruption. And there is also no impossibility in their view in its continuation thus, nor in the continuation of the types of compounds within their successive individuals ad infinitum.

Commentary
This passage presents the second division of bodies – that they are eternal in both essence and attributes. The author attributes this view to Aristotle and his later followers, al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. The exposition then provides a detailed breakdown of the philosophers' position by dividing bodies into celestial (falakiyyāt) and elemental (ʿunṣuriyyāt). For celestial bodies, they are eternal in matter, corporeal form, specific form, and determinate accidents (quantities, shapes, etc.) – except for individual motions (ḥarakāt shakhṣiyya) and positions (awḍāʿ), which are originated. The reason given is that each individual motion is preceded by another to infinity, so there cannot be a first motion. However, motion in the absolute sense (muṭlaq al-ḥaraka) and position (al-waḍʿ) are eternal because the celestial spheres move continuously from pre‑eternity to post‑eternity without ever ceasing. For elemental bodies, the philosophers hold them eternal in matter and in corporeal form by type (bi‑nawʿihā), because matter always has a corporeal form of a single type, and the type persists through successive individuals. They are also eternal in specific forms by genus (bi‑jinsihā), because matter must always have some specific form, and these specific forms share a genus that persists through successive types. However, the individualizing forms (al-ṣuwar al-mushakhkhiṣa) in both corporeal and specific forms, as well as the determinate accidents, are originated. The author adds that there is no contradiction in the view that some elemental specific forms (like the human type) may be originated and not continuously existent, as they could arise from another element via generation and corruption. Similarly, the continuation of such types or the types of compounds within successive individuals ad infinitum is possible in their doctrine. This detailed exposition clarifies the nuanced stance of the Peripatetic philosophers.

The Impossibility of Eternal Individualizing Forms
Commentator argues against eternal individualizing forms using infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why individualizing forms cannot be eternal by their type or genus, due to the logical problem of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Eternal individualizing forms lead to an infinite regress, which is impossible.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key point that is often misunderstood: how can specific forms be eternal? He argues that if an individualizing form were eternal by its type or genus, it would require another form to individualize it, and so on indefinitely. This sets the stage for understanding the limits of eternity in physical forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the passage and find the statement about the impossibility of individualizing forms being eternal by type or genus. Notice the reasoning: if they were eternal, they would need further individualization.
2
Understand the infinite regress
Trace the logical chain: each individualizing form would require another individualizing form, leading to an infinite sequence (tasalsul). Why is this problematic in Islamic theology?
3
Contrast with the alternative
The commentator mentions that the only conceivable way for such a form to be eternal is by a 'general accident' (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm), but he dismisses this as meaningless. Reflect on why that is not a satisfactory solution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (نعم الصور المشخصة فيهما هذا من تتمة قوله : أنهم قالوا إلخ ولا يذهب عليك ) أن الصورة المشخصة لا يتصور أن يكون قديمة بنوعها أو بجنسها وإلا لكانت محتاجة إلى صورة أخرى مشخصة أو منوعة وهلم جراً فيلزم التسلسل وإنما المتصور هو أن يكون قديمة بالعرض العام ولا عبرة بذلك . قوله : ( كأن يكون نوع الإنسان حادثاً مع أن نوع الإنسان كان قديماً عندهم وأن امتزاجه كانت مركبة من العناصر الأربعة فيلزم أن يكون نوعاً النار في ضمن تلك الأفراد قديماً عندهم، هذا ولعلهم أرادوا بحدوثه حدوث كرة النار بحركات الأفلاك بطريق الكون والفساد وأن هذه الكرة مخالفة بالنوع للأجزاء النارية التي في المركبات أو ردوا بقولهم : وبصروها النوعية بجنسها أن المقطوع عندهم هو أن يكون الصور النوعية للعنصريات قديمة بجنسها لا أن يكون قديمة بأنواعها على ما يشعر به قول (المصنف) ولا امتناع في حدوثه إلخ. وكذا قول الشارح: ولا امتناع أيضاً عندهم .
His statement: 'Indeed, the individualizing forms in both' – this is part of the completion of his statement that they said etc. And let it not be hidden from you that the individualizing form cannot be conceived to be eternal by its type or by its genus, for otherwise it would need another individualizing or specific form, and so on, leading to an infinite regress. What can be conceived is that it is eternal by a general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm), and no attention is paid to that. His statement: 'As for example the human type being originated' – even though the human type was eternal in their view, and its mixture was composed of the four elements, it would follow that the type of fire within those individuals would be eternal in their view. This – and perhaps they intended by its origination the origination of the sphere of fire by the motions of the celestial spheres through generation and corruption, and that this sphere is different in type from the fiery parts in compounds. Or they rejected by their statement: 'and their specific forms by their genus' that what is certain in their view is that the specific forms of elemental bodies are eternal by their genus, not that they are eternal by their types, as indicated by the statement of the author: 'and there is no impossibility in its origination' etc. Similarly the statement of the commentator: 'and there is also no impossibility in their view.'
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage contains two comments (qālahu) from the commentator on the main text. The first comment explains the phrase 'indeed, the individualizing forms in both' from the earlier discussion of celestial and elemental bodies. The commentator clarifies that an individualizing form (ṣūra mushakhkhiṣa) cannot be eternal by its type (nawʿ) or genus (jins), because that would require an infinite regress of further individualizing forms. The only conceivable way for such a form to be eternal is by a general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm), but that is not meaningful. The second comment addresses the example of the human type being originated. The commentator notes that if the human type were originated, it might seem to contradict the philosophers' view that the human type is eternal. He explains that the philosophers may mean by 'origination' the origination of the sphere of fire (kurat al-nār) through celestial motions via generation and corruption, which is a different type from the fire particles in compounds. Alternatively, the philosophers' statement that 'their specific forms by their genus' indicates that elemental specific forms are eternal by genus, not by individual types, so a new type like human could originate within that genus without contradicting the principle of eternal generic forms. The commentator concludes by noting that the author and the commentator both assert that there is no impossibility in such origination.

Key Terms
الصور المشخصة
التسلسل
العرض العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Individualizing forms can be eternal in themselves without any further cause.
Correction: If a form were eternal by its own type, it would still require individualization, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Thus such forms cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
Why can an individualizing form not be eternal by its type or genus?
A) Because it would be too complex
B) Because it would require an infinite regress of forms
C) Because the author said so
D) Because it contradicts the Torah

Key Takeaway
Individualizing forms cannot be eternal by type or genus because that leads to an infinite regress, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the infinite regress argument, ask yourself: in what areas of your own life do you rely on an endless chain of justifications (e.g., 'I'll pray later when I'm less busy, and later when that's less busy...') rather than stopping at a firm foundation? What is that foundation for you?
?
This passage shows that some things cannot be pushed back infinitely. Is there a belief or habit in your relationship with Allah that you have been postponing indefinitely, assuming it will somehow resolve itself? What concrete step will you take today to end that regression?
?
The commentator dismisses the idea of eternity by a general accident as irrelevant. Are there 'general accidents' in your daily life—things you treat as important but are really just distractions from what is essential? How can you refocus on what truly matters for your akhira?

The Origin of the Human Type and Eternal Genera
Commentator resolves the apparent conflict between the origination of the human type and the eternity of specific forms by genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the human type can be originated while maintaining that specific forms are eternal by genus.

Core Idea
A new species can arise within an eternal genus without contradiction.

This passage addresses an apparent tension: if the human type is originated, does that contradict the philosophers' view that specific forms are eternal? The commentator offers two explanations: the origination refers to the sphere of fire, or the eternity is by genus, not by species. This clarifies how new types can emerge without negating the eternal nature of generic forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Read the passage and note that the human type is said to be originated, yet philosophers held that specific forms are eternal. What seems to conflict?
2
Examine the first explanation
The commentator suggests that 'origination' might refer to the sphere of fire produced by celestial motions. How does this avoid the contradiction?
3
Examine the second explanation
Alternatively, the philosophers may mean that specific forms are eternal by genus, not by species. How does this allow for a new species like the human type?
4
Conclude
Both explanations show that origination of a new type does not negate the eternity of the broader genus. This harmonizes the positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (نعم الصور المشخصة فيهما هذا من تتمة قوله : أنهم قالوا إلخ ولا يذهب عليك ) أن الصورة المشخصة لا يتصور أن يكون قديمة بنوعها أو بجنسها وإلا لكانت محتاجة إلى صورة أخرى مشخصة أو منوعة وهلم جراً فيلزم التسلسل وإنما المتصور هو أن يكون قديمة بالعرض العام ولا عبرة بذلك . قوله : ( كأن يكون نوع الإنسان حادثاً مع أن نوع الإنسان كان قديماً عندهم وأن امتزاجه كانت مركبة من العناصر الأربعة فيلزم أن يكون نوعاً النار في ضمن تلك الأفراد قديماً عندهم، هذا ولعلهم أرادوا بحدوثه حدوث كرة النار بحركات الأفلاك بطريق الكون والفساد وأن هذه الكرة مخالفة بالنوع للأجزاء النارية التي في المركبات أو ردوا بقولهم : وبصروها النوعية بجنسها أن المقطوع عندهم هو أن يكون الصور النوعية للعنصريات قديمة بجنسها لا أن يكون قديمة بأنواعها على ما يشعر به قول (المصنف) ولا امتناع في حدوثه إلخ. وكذا قول الشارح: ولا امتناع أيضاً عندهم .
His statement: 'Indeed, the individualizing forms in both' – this is part of the completion of his statement that they said etc. And let it not be hidden from you that the individualizing form cannot be conceived to be eternal by its type or by its genus, for otherwise it would need another individualizing or specific form, and so on, leading to an infinite regress. What can be conceived is that it is eternal by a general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm), and no attention is paid to that. His statement: 'As for example the human type being originated' – even though the human type was eternal in their view, and its mixture was composed of the four elements, it would follow that the type of fire within those individuals would be eternal in their view. This – and perhaps they intended by its origination the origination of the sphere of fire by the motions of the celestial spheres through generation and corruption, and that this sphere is different in type from the fiery parts in compounds. Or they rejected by their statement: 'and their specific forms by their genus' that what is certain in their view is that the specific forms of elemental bodies are eternal by their genus, not that they are eternal by their types, as indicated by the statement of the author: 'and there is no impossibility in its origination' etc. Similarly the statement of the commentator: 'and there is also no impossibility in their view.'
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage contains two comments (qālahu) from the commentator on the main text. The first comment explains the phrase 'indeed, the individualizing forms in both' from the earlier discussion of celestial and elemental bodies. The commentator clarifies that an individualizing form (ṣūra mushakhkhiṣa) cannot be eternal by its type (nawʿ) or genus (jins), because that would require an infinite regress of further individualizing forms. The only conceivable way for such a form to be eternal is by a general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm), but that is not meaningful. The second comment addresses the example of the human type being originated. The commentator notes that if the human type were originated, it might seem to contradict the philosophers' view that the human type is eternal. He explains that the philosophers may mean by 'origination' the origination of the sphere of fire (kurat al-nār) through celestial motions via generation and corruption, which is a different type from the fire particles in compounds. Alternatively, the philosophers' statement that 'their specific forms by their genus' indicates that elemental specific forms are eternal by genus, not by individual types, so a new type like human could originate within that genus without contradicting the principle of eternal generic forms. The commentator concludes by noting that the author and the commentator both assert that there is no impossibility in such origination.

Key Terms
نوع الإنسان
كرة النار
الكون والفساد
الصور النوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers believed that no new species can ever come into existence.
Correction: The passage shows that they allowed new species to originate as long as the broader genus remains eternal, or through celestial processes like generation and corruption.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how can the human type be originated without contradicting the eternity of specific forms?
A) By denying that the human type is a specific form
B) By interpreting 'origination' as the sphere of fire or by limiting eternity to genus
C) By claiming the human type is not really originated
D) By rejecting the philosophers' view

Key Takeaway
The human type can be originated because specific forms are eternal by genus, not by species, allowing new types to emerge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argued that the genus of a thing can be eternal even if its specific form is originated. Have you ever assumed that something in your life—like a habit, a relationship, or a belief—is fixed and unchangeable, only to realize that its 'genus' (core essence) can remain while its specific expression can be renewed? What would it take for you to intentionally allow a new 'specific form' to emerge in that area?
?
This lesson shows that apparent contradictions can be resolved by more precise understanding of terms. Think of a time when you felt torn between two conflicting ideas about your faith or your life. How might a deeper clarification of key concepts—like what you mean by 'eternal' or 'originated'—reveal that they are actually compatible? What step can you take today to pursue that clarity?

Al-Razi's Argument from Composition and Possibility
Al-Razi presents his fourth proof that every body is possible because composite, leading to its origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the composition of bodies to prove that they are possible and therefore created, and how this relates to the nature of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Composite things cannot be necessary, so bodies are possible and must be produced from non-existence.

Al-Razi's fourth proof for the temporality of bodies is a concise yet powerful argument. He begins with a simple observation: every body is composite. From this, he deduces that bodies cannot be necessary existents, which are necessarily one and non-composite. Instead, they are possible entities that require a producer, and production implies origination from non-existence. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of this proof and its implications for theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once in Arabic, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from composition to possibility to origination.
Pay attention to the two possible types of composition mentioned.
2
Identify the key premise
Al-Razi starts with 'kull jism mumkin li'annahu murakkab'. Understand why composition is used as evidence for possibility.
He mentions two philosophical views on composition: atoms or hyle and form.
3
Contrast with the Necessary Existent
The proof hinges on the nature of the Necessary Existent as one and non-composite. Reflect on why this contrast is essential.
4
Trace the conclusion
From possibility, the argument moves to production and non-existence. See how the author links the proof to earlier discussions.
This step connects to the broader kalam doctrine of creation.
5
Reflect on al-Razi's principle
The author reminds us of al-Razi's stance on the eternal not depending on a cause. Consider how this supports the proof.
This is a key theological nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الرابع له أيضاً كل جسم ممكن لأنه مركب إما من الجواهر الفردة أو الهيولى والصورة وكثير أي وتشاركه في ماهيته أمور متعددة وسيأتي في الإلهيات أن الواجب الوجود واحد لا شريك له في حقيقته وغير مركب فلا يكون الجسم واجباً بل ممكناً وكل ممكن هو موجد فله ولا يتصور الإيجاد إلا عن عدم وهو مبني على ما ذكرنا في مباحث القدم من لا يجوز الإمام الرازي استناد القديم إلى السبب الموجب كما لم يجوزوا استناده إلى المختار وقد نبهناك على مأخذه فتذكره
The fourth way also for him: Every body is possible because it is composite – either from indivisible atoms or from hyle and form – and many, i.e., multiple things share in its essence. It will come in the Divine Matters that the Necessary Existent is one, having no partner in His reality, and is not composite. So the body is not necessary but possible. And every possible is produced (mujad) and has a producer, and production is inconceivable except from non-existence. This is based on what we mentioned in the discussions of the eternal: that al-Razi does not allow the eternal to depend on a necessitating cause, just as they do not allow its dependence on a free agent. And we have alerted you to his principle, so remember it.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the temporality of bodies, also attributed to Imam al-Razi. The argument is straightforward: every body is composite, either from indivisible atoms (jawahir fardah) or from hyle and form (hayula wa surah), and composition implies sharing in essence with multiple things. The Necessary Existent (al-wajib al-wujud) is one and non-composite, as will be discussed in theology. Therefore, a body cannot be necessary (wajib) but is possible (mumkin). Everything possible is produced (mujud) and has a producer, and production (ijad) is inconceivable except from non-existence (adam). Thus, bodies are originated. The argument rests on the premise that an eternal being cannot depend on a cause, whether necessitating or free, as al-Razi held. The author reminds the reader to recall al-Razi's principle from earlier discussions.

Al-Razi's proof shows that the composite nature of bodies entails their possibility and thus their origination from non-existence, consistent with the oneness and simplicity of the Necessary Existent.

Al-Razi's Third Proof: Motion or Rest in Pre-Eternity
Al-Razi's proof from motion/rest impossibility in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that an eternal body cannot be either moving or at rest in pre-eternity, and why that refutes the existence of an eternal body.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are accidents that require a beginning, so no body can be eternal.

This lesson unpacks Imām al-Rāzī's third proof for the originatedness of bodies. Building on earlier arguments, al-Rāzī presents a concise disjunction: any eternal body would be either moving or at rest in pre-eternity. Both options are impossible, so an eternal body cannot exist. The author's brief treatment assumes familiarity with earlier elaborations, so we will clarify the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: introduction, attribution, and the core argument.
The author references earlier proofs, so understanding the context helps.
2
Identify the key disjunction
The proof hinges on the statement that an eternal body would be either moving or at rest in pre-eternity. Identify this disjunction and note that both options are declared impossible.
3
Understand why both are impossible
Recall from earlier lessons that motion and rest are accidents (a'rāḍ). Accidents are contingent and require a cause; they cannot be eternal. Therefore, neither motion nor rest can exist in pre-eternity without a beginning.
This reasoning is assumed from the first and second proofs.
4
Trace the logical conclusion
Since an eternal body would necessarily have one of these accidents, and both are impossible, the premise that an eternal body exists is false. Therefore, all bodies are originated (ḥādith).
5
Reflect on the author's brevity
Notice that the author does not elaborate because the explanation was given in the two previous paths. This shows how the arguments build on each other.
Consider the pedagogical value of repetition and conciseness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثالث للإمام الرازي: ذكره في المحصل ونسبه الآمدي إلى بعض المتأخرين من الأشاعرة وهو أيضاً مأخوذ من المسلك الأول والمؤونات التي كانت فيه باقية هاهنا بحالها سوى قليل منها كما لا يخفى وتقريره أنه لو وجد جسم قديم لكان في الأزل إما متحركاً أو ساكناً والتالي باطل بقسميه وأنت بمعرفة بيانه بعد ما قررناه في المسلكين السابقين خبير فلا نشتغل حذفاً للمؤونة
The third path (maslak) of Imām al-Rāzī: he mentioned it in al-Muḥaṣṣal and al-Āmidī attributed it to some later Ashʿarīs. It is also taken from the first path, and the burdens that were in it remain here as they were, except for a few of them, as is not hidden. Its formulation is that if an eternal body existed, then in pre-eternity it would be either moving or at rest, and the consequent is false in both divisions. And you, after what we established in the two previous paths, are experienced in its explanation, so we do not occupy ourselves with it in order to reduce the burden.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's third proof for the origination of bodies. The author notes that al-Razi mentioned it in his work al-Muḥaṣṣal, and al-Āmidi attributes it to some later Ash'aris. The proof is derived from the first path, with most of its complications remaining. The core argument is: if a body were eternal, then in pre-eternity it would have to be either in motion or at rest. Both options are impossible: motion and rest each require a beginning (since they are accidents), so an eternal body cannot have either. Therefore, an eternal body cannot exist. The author does not elaborate further because the reader already knows the explanation from the previous two paths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this proof requires motion or rest to be essential to bodies; it only considers their contingent states.
Correction: The proof does not assume that motion or rest are essential attributes of bodies; it simply notes that at any given moment, a body must be in one of these two accidental states. It is the impossibility of an eternal accident that makes both states impossible in pre-eternity.

Checkpoint

D) A body either has a beginning or is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of motion or rest in pre-eternity entails that no body can be eternal, as any body must be either moving or at rest at any given moment.

?
You have just considered a logical argument that demands a beginning for everything except Allah. How does confronting your own beginning—that you were created from nothing—affect your sense of accountability to your Creator? What will you change today because of that awareness?

The Fourth Proof: Bodies are Possible Due to Composition
Al-Rāzī's fourth proof: bodies are possible because they are composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Rāzī's fourth proof that every body is possible because it is composite, and how this implies the necessity of a creator and creation from non-existence.

Core Idea
Compositeness implies possibility; the Necessary Existent is incomposite.

This lesson presents al-Rāzī's fourth argument for the origination of bodies. It argues that since every body is composite, it cannot be necessary; only the incomposite Necessary Existent is necessary. The proof then connects possibility to the need for a creator, emphasizing that creation is ex nihilo.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the proof.
The passage is dense; take time to understand each phrase.
2
Identify the two types of composition
The proof mentions that bodies are composite either from indivisible atoms or from matter and form. Recognize these as the two main theories of material composition in kalām.
This sets up the basis for possibility.
3
Connect composition to possibility
Notice how the author links composition to possibility by referencing the Necessary Existent's incompositeness. This is the key logical step.
The Necessary Existent cannot be composite, so composite things cannot be necessary.
4
Follow the implications for creation
The author states that every possible existent requires a creator and that creation is only conceivable from non-existence. Reflect on why this matters for the proof of God's existence.
This ties the proof to the broader discussion of pre-eternity and causation.
5
Recall the background discussion
The passage refers to earlier investigations about pre-eternity and al-Rāzī's position on eternal causation. Review that context if needed.
This strengthens the proof's foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الرابع له أيضاً كل جسم ممكن لأنه مركب إما من الجواهر الفردة أو الهيولى والصورة وكثير أي وتشاركه في ماهيته أمور متعددة وسيأتي في الإلهيات أن الواجب الوجود واحد لا شريك له في حقيقته وغير مركب فلا يكون الجسم واجباً بل ممكناً وكل ممكن هو موجد فله ولا يتصور الإيجاد إلا عن عدم وهو مبني على ما ذكرنا في مباحث القدم من لا يجوز الإمام الرازي استناد القديم إلى السبب الموجب كما لم يجوزوا استناده إلى المختار وقد نبهناك على مأخذه فتذكره
The fourth path (maslak) of his (i.e., al-Rāzī) also: every body is possible because it is composite, either from simple substances (jawāhir farda) or from matter (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra). And 'many' means that multiple things share in its quiddity (māhiyya). Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the origination of bodies, also attributable to al-Razi. The proof argues that every body is possible (mumkin) because it is composite: either it is composed of indivisible atoms (jawahir farda) or of matter (hayula) and form (sura). Compositeness indicates that the body's quiddity (mahiyya) is shared by multiple components, which is a sign of contingency. The author then refers to a later discussion in the section on divine attributes (al-ilahiyyat) where it will be shown that the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) is One, incomposite, and has no partner in His reality. Since the Necessary Existent is not composite, a body cannot be necessary; therefore it is possible. Every possible existent requires a creator (mujid), and creation (ijad) is only conceivable from non-existence, i.e., it must be originated. This proof builds on earlier discussions about pre-eternity: al-Razi does not allow the eternal to be caused by a necessitating cause, just as he does not allow it to be caused by a free agent. The reader is reminded to recall the source of this argument.

Fifth and Sixth Proofs: Free Agency and Accidents
Author presents fifth and sixth proofs for body origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth and sixth proofs for the origin of bodies, their premises, and their limited scope (only bodies and their attributes, not separate substances).

Core Idea
Two proofs for bodies' origin: free agency and being loci of accidents.

The author presents two additional proofs for the origin of bodies. The fifth proof argues from free agency, the sixth from the observation of accidents in bodies. However, these proofs have limitations: they only prove the origin of bodies and their attributes, not of separate substances. This lesson unpacks these arguments and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the author introduces two proofs and then comments on their scope.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يحتاج في تعميمها إلى نفي المجردات'.
2
Identify the two proofs
The fifth proof: bodies are the act of a free agent. The sixth proof: bodies are loci of originated accidents. Understand the basic premises of each.
The free agent argument is deferred to the section on attributes; the accident argument is based on observation.
3
Understand the limitations
The author notes that the fourth and fifth proofs together only give the origin of bodies and their attributes, not the entire world (including separate substances). The sixth proof is limited similarly.
This corrects a potential misconception that these proofs prove everything.
4
Why the third proof was omitted
The author says the third proof is essentially the same as the first due to 'persistence of the burdens'. Reflect on what this might mean.
This is a technical point about overlapping arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الخامس: الأجسام فعل الفاعل المختار لما سيأتي في الصفات أي في صفاته تعالى فتكون الأجسام حادثة لما بينا أن القديم لا يستند إلى المختار وهذا الوجهان أي الرابع والخامس يثبتان حدوث العالم كله من الأجسام والمجردات وصفاتهما بخلاف الأولين كذلك فإنهما لا يعطيان إلا حدوث الأجسام وصفاتها ويحتاج في تعميمها إلى نفي المجردات ولم يتعرض للمسلك الثالث لأنه جعله عين الأول لبقاء المؤونات وأما السادس فهو في حكم الأولين بلا اشتباه المسلك السادس: الجسم يقوم به الحادث وهو ضروري لما نشاهده من حدوث الحركات القائمة به وتجدد الأعراض الحالة فيه كالأضواء والألوان والأشكال وغيرها ولا شيء من القديم لما سنبرهن عليه في الإلهيات من أن القديم لا يكون محلا للحوادث
The fifth path (maslak): bodies are the act of the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār), due to what will come in the [section on] attributes, i.e., in His, exalted, attributes. So bodies are originated because we have shown that the eternal is not attributed to the free agent. And these two ways, i.e., the fourth and fifth, prove the origination of the entire world, including bodies, separate substances, and their attributes, unlike the first two [paths]. Likewise, they only give the origination of bodies and their attributes and need in generalizing them to deny separate substances. And he did not mention the third [path] because he considered it the same as the first due to the persistence of the burdens. And as for the sixth, it is in the same rule as the first two without doubt. The sixth path: the body is a locus (maḥall) for the originated accident (al-ḥādith), and this is necessary because of what we observe of the origination of motions subsisting in it and the renewal of accidents inhabiting it, such as lights, colors, shapes, and others. And nothing of the eternal [is a locus for accidents] as we will prove in the divine matters (al-ilāhiyyāt) that the eternal cannot be a locus for originated things.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and sixth proofs for the origination of bodies. The fifth proof argues that bodies are the act of a free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār). This argument will be developed later in the section on divine attributes, but the gist is that the eternal cannot be attributed to a free agent because that would imply a temporal beginning. The author notes that the fourth and fifth proofs together prove the origination of the entire world, including bodies, separate substances (mujarradāt) such as souls and intellects, and their attributes—unlike the first two proofs which only prove the origination of bodies. However, upon further reflection, the author clarifies that actually these two proofs only give the origination of bodies and their attributes, and to generalize them to the whole world one would need to deny the existence of separate substances (which are not bodies). The author also explains why the third proof was not mentioned: because it is essentially the same as the first proof due to the persistence of its burdensome assumptions. The sixth proof is said to be in the same ruling as the first two: it only proves the origination of bodies. The sixth proof itself is that the body is a locus (maḥall) for originated accidents (ḥawādith). We observe that bodies undergo motion and accidents like lights and colors. Since the eternal cannot be a locus for accidents (as will be proved later), bodies must be originated.

Key Terms

قديم
حادث
محل للحوادث
حركة
عرض
مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fifth and sixth proofs prove the origin of the entire world, including separate substances.
Correction: The author explicitly states that these proofs only give the origin of bodies and their attributes; to include separate substances requires additional arguments denying their existence.

Checkpoint

A) They only prove the origin of bodies and their attributes, not separate substances.
B) They rely on the existence of free will which is controversial.

D) They are based on observation, which is uncertain.

Are you living as if your life is a random accident or a deliberate gift? What would it look like to respond to that gift with gratitude and responsibility starting now?

The Counterexample of the Daily Event
Counterexample and defense against the first objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the daily event serves as a counterexample to the opponent's proof for the world's eternity and how the author defends it against the objection of permissible successive chains.

Core Idea
If the opponent's reasoning were correct, even a daily event would be eternal.

The author presents the first reliable answer to the doubt about the world's temporal origination. He introduces a counterexample: the daily event. If the opponent's argument were sound, it would force us to conclude that any temporal event—like the daily rising of the sun—must be eternal. That is clearly false. This lesson walks through that counterexample, the anticipated objection about infinite chains of successive events, and the author's rebuttal that both simultaneous and successive chains are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage through
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: counterexample, objection, reply.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the counterexample
Find where the author introduces the daily event (al-hadith al-yawmi). Understand why it is a valid counterexample: if the opponent's proof were sound, it would wrongly imply that the daily event is eternal.
The daily event is a known temporal event, so concluding it is eternal would be absurd.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent objects that the daily event depends on an infinite chain of celestial events, which is permissible. Note the distinction they make between simultaneous and successive chains.
The opponent claims that a chain of successive events is acceptable, unlike a chain of simultaneous causes.
4
Follow the author's reply
The author argues that merely pointing out the difference does not defeat the counterexample, because an infinite chain—whether simultaneous or successive—is impossible. This is a key principle.
The author insists that the impossibility of infinite regress applies to both types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر في الجواب عنه وجوه والذي يصلح للتعويل عليه وجهان : الأول : النقض بالحادث اليومي ) إذ لا شبهة في وجوده فنقول : فاعلية الفاعل القديم لهذا الحادث قديمة إذ لو كانت حادثة لوقفت على شرط حادث حذراً من الترجيح بلا مرجح والكلام في هذا الشرط الحادث كما في الأول فتتسلسل الحوادث المترتبة إلى ما لا نهاية له فلو صح دليلكم لكان الحادث اليومي قديماً ( لا يقال : إنه ) أي الحادث اليومي ( يستند إلى الحوادث الفلكية من الحركات والاتصالات الكوكبية ( وكل منها مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية ومثل هذا التسلسل جائز بخلاف التسلسل في الأمور المترتبة المجتمعة ) لأنا نقول : ابتداء الفارق ) بين صورة النقض ومحل النزاع على الوجه الذي ذكرتموه ( لا يدفع النقض ) لأن التسلسل في الأمور التي ضبطها وجود سواء كانت مجتمعة أو متعاقبة محال كما وقفت عليه . ( وأيضاً فنقول : ( إذا سلم جواز التسلسل في الحوادث المتعاقبة ( فلم لا يجوز أن يكون حدوث العالم مشروطا بشرط مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية فيكون حدوث العالم عن المبدأ القديم بتسلسل الحوادث المتعاقبة كما في الحادث اليومي عندكم ) فإن قيل : ذلك ) أي تسلسل الشروط المتعاقبة ( إنما يتصور فيما له مادة بتزايد استعدادها بتوارد تلك الشروط عليها لقبول الحادث المشروط بتلك الشروط حتى إذا كمل الاستعداد فاض عليها من المبدأ القديم ما هي مستعدة له ( وما سوى العالم ) أي ما هو خارج عنه (ليس له, مادة حتى يتصور توارد الشروط المعتبرة في حدوث العالم عليها قلنا: لا نسلم ذلك ) الذي ذكرتموه من أن الشروط والحوادث المتعاقبة إنما يتصور في الماديات (إذ قد تكون تصورات متعاقبة لأمر (مجرد) عن المادة وتوابعها ( كل سابق منها شرط للاحق إلى أن تنتهي فيما يزال إلى ما هو (شرط) أي إلى تصور هو شرط لحدوث العالم) الجسماني فلا الاستدال بما ذكرتم على قدمه إلا أن يقال : لكل حادث مادة ) وتلك المادة لا تخلو عن الصورة ( فيكون هذا رجوعا إلى الطريقة الأولى وقد أجبنا عنها ) .
And responses to it have been mentioned, and the two that are reliable are two: First: the refutation by the daily event, for there is no doubt about its existence. So we say: The agency of the eternal agent for this event is eternal, because if it were temporal, it would depend on a temporal condition, to avoid preponderance without a preponderant, and the discussion about this temporal condition is like the first, so temporal events would chain in succession ad infinitum. If your proof were valid, the daily event would be eternal. It cannot be said that the daily event is based on celestial events—movements and planetary conjunctions—each preceded by another ad infinitum, and such a chain is permissible, unlike the chain in simultaneous sequential things. For we say: Simply mentioning the difference between the form of the refutation and the point of dispute in the way you mentioned does not repel the refutation, because the chain in things whose existence is conditioned—whether simultaneous or successive—is impossible, as you have seen. Also, we say: If the permissibility of the chain in successive events is granted, then why is it not permissible for the occurrence of the world to be conditioned by a condition preceded by another ad infinitum, so that the occurrence of the world from the eternal principle would be through a chain of successive events, as in the daily event according to you? If it is said that such a chain of successive conditions is only conceivable in something that has a matter whose preparedness increases through the succession of those conditions upon it to receive the event conditioned by those conditions, until when the preparedness is complete, what it is prepared for emanates upon it from the eternal principle; and that which is other than the world—i.e., what is outside it—has no matter such that the succession of conditions considered in the occurrence of the world upon it could be conceived. We say: We do not accept that which you have mentioned—that conditions and successive events are only conceivable in material things—for there may be successive concepts for an immaterial thing, free from matter and its concomitants, each preceding one being a condition for the subsequent one until it ends eventually in what is a condition, i.e., a concept that is a condition for the occurrence of the corporeal world. So the inference from what you mentioned for its eternity is invalid, unless it is said: Every temporal event has a matter, and that matter is not free from form. But this is a return to the first method, and we have answered it.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two reliable answers to a doubt (likely concerning the temporal origination of the world). The author begins by stating that among the responses to the doubt, two are reliable. The first answer is a counterexample (naqd) using the daily event (al-hadith al-yawmi)—i.e., a temporal event that occurs every day, such as the rising of the sun. The argument is: The agency (fa'iliyya) of the eternal agent (al-fa'il al-qadim) for this daily event is eternal, because if it were temporal, it would require a temporal condition to avoid the problem of preponderance without a preponderant (tarjih bila murajjih). That condition would then be another temporal event, leading to an infinite chain of temporal events (tasalsul al-hawadith al-mutarattiba). If the opponent's proof (that the world must be eternal) were valid, it would imply that the daily event is also eternal, which is false. Then the author anticipates an objection: The daily event is not directly from the eternal agent but depends on celestial events (movements, planetary conjunctions), which themselves form an infinite chain of successive events, and this type of chain is permissible, unlike a chain of simultaneous sequential things. The author replies that merely pointing out this difference does not defuse the counterexample, because an infinite chain—whether of simultaneous or successive things—is impossible (muhal) as established earlier. Next, a further argument is made: If a chain of successive events is granted as permissible, then why not allow the occurrence of the world to be conditioned by an infinite chain of successive conditions, so that the world's creation from the eternal principle (al-mabda' al-qadim) would involve a succession of events, just like the daily event according to the opponent? The opponent might retort that such successive conditions apply only to material things (maddiyat) whose preparedness (isti'dad) increases through the succession of conditions until a certain point, and immaterial things (such as the world outside matter) have no matter to receive such conditions. The author rejects this restriction, arguing that there can be successive concepts (tassawurat muta'aqiba) for an immaterial thing (amr mujarrad), each preceding concept being a condition for the subsequent one, eventually leading to a condition for the corporeal world. Thus the opponent cannot rely on the material/immaterial distinction. Finally, the opponent might fall back on the claim that every temporal event must have matter, and matter is never free from form, but this reverts to an earlier argument (al-tariqa al-ula) which has already been answered. The passage ends there, leaving the first answer complete.

Key Terms
نقض
حادث يومي
تسلسل
حوادث متعاقبة
حوادث مترتبة مجتمعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an infinite chain of successive events is permissible while a simultaneous chain is not, and that this difference saves the opponent's argument.
Correction: The author argues that both types of infinite chains are impossible when existence is conditioned, so the distinction does not refute the counterexample.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the 'daily event' (al-hadith al-yawmi) in this argument?
A) To prove that the world is eternal.
B) To serve as a counterexample showing that the opponent's proof would wrongly render a known temporal event eternal.
C) To demonstrate that celestial movements are eternal.
D) To argue that agency of the eternal agent cannot be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The daily event counterexample shows that the opponent's reasoning, if sound, would lead to an absurd conclusion, thus it is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the agency behind even the most mundane daily event is eternal and direct from Allah, what does that mean for your own sense of self-sufficiency? Do you truly believe that every breath you take is sustained by His eternal will, or do you live as if some things are automatic? What one habit could you change to make this belief real in your life today?
?
The opponent's error was failing to follow their own reasoning to its logical conclusion. Is there a belief you hold about your own life—perhaps about your independence, your achievements, or your control—that, if followed to its end, would reveal a contradiction? Are you ready to accept the truth even if it humbles you?
?
The counterexample shows that rejecting an infinite chain forces us to affirm an eternal cause. Does your daily reliance on created means (people, systems, your own efforts) ever obscure your awareness that Allah is the ultimate and immediate cause of everything? How can you train your heart to see His agency behind every 'daily event' in your life?

Refuting the Matter-Based Restriction
Second objection and reply regarding material conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that conditions and successive events only apply to material things, and the author's reply using successive immaterial concepts as conditions.

Core Idea
Even immaterial realities can have successive conditions, refuting the opponent's restriction to matter.

After defending the counterexample against the first objection, the author proceeds to address another objection: the opponent argues that an infinite chain of successive conditions only works for material things that undergo preparedness. The author counters by giving an example of successive concepts for an immaterial thing, each concept being a condition for the next, ultimately leading to the corporeal world's condition. This undermines the opponent's attempt to limit the argument to material entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Start from 'if it is said that such a chain...' and read through to the end. Notice the objection and the author's two-part reply.
This part builds on the earlier argument.
2
Identify the objection about matter and preparedness
The opponent claims that successive conditions apply only to things with matter that gradually becomes prepared. This would exclude the world (which is immaterial) from such a chain.
The world 'outside matter' supposedly cannot have successive conditions.
3
Understand the author's first reply: immaterial concepts
The author rejects the restriction, giving the example of successive concepts for an immaterial thing (amr mujarrad). Each preceding concept is a condition for the next, until a final concept conditions the corporeal world.
This shows that immaterial things can have a chain of conditions.
4
Consider the opponent's fallback and the author's dismissal
The opponent might revert to saying every temporal event has matter and matter is never free from form. But the author notes this is a return to an earlier argument already answered.
The author does not rehash the earlier reply here; he simply points out the repetition.

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two reliable answers to a doubt (likely concerning the temporal origination of the world). The author begins by stating that among the responses to the doubt, two are reliable. The first answer is a counterexample (naqd) using the daily event (al-hadith al-yawmi)—i.e., a temporal event that occurs every day, such as the rising of the sun. That condition would then be another temporal event, leading to an infinite chain of temporal events (tasalsul al-hawadith al-mutarattiba). If the opponent's proof (that the world must be eternal) were valid, it would imply that the daily event is also eternal, which is false. Then the author anticipates an objection: The daily event is not directly from the eternal agent but depends on celestial events (movements, planetary conjunctions), which themselves form an infinite chain of successive events, and this type of chain is permissible, unlike a chain of simultaneous sequential things. Finally, the opponent might fall back on the claim that every temporal event must have matter, and matter is never free from form, but this reverts to an earlier argument (al-tariqa al-ula) which has already been answered. The passage ends there, leaving the first answer complete.

Preponderance by Mere Will: The Free Agent's Choice
Explanation of the second reliable answer: preponderance by mere will, with objection and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the free agent's preponderance of one potential object over another is explained solely by mere will, without requiring an external motive or preponderant, and how objections regarding eternity and infinite regress are answered.

Core Idea
The free agent's choice is explained by mere will, not by a preponderant cause.

This lesson presents the second reliable answer to the doubt about the temporal origination of the world from an eternal agent. The author argues that the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) preponderates one of his potential objects over the other solely by mere will, without needing any additional motive or preponderant. He then addresses a key objection concerning the eternity of the will and potential infinite regress, offering two solutions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the author's direct claim that preponderance is by mere will.
The example of the two paths and the thirsty man illustrates the point.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: if the will is eternal, the effect would be eternal; if temporal, it needs another will or cause, leading to infinite regress.
This is a classic objection against temporal origination from an eternal agent.
3
Examine the two answers
The author provides two answers: (1) succession of multiple temporal wills or relations of a single eternal will ad infinitum, (2) the relation of will to its object is a mental consideration that can occur at a specific time without a specifying cause.
The second answer relies on the concept of 'amr i'tibari' (mental consideration).
4
Reflect on the closing exhortation
The author urges careful reflection and steadfastness, as these matters are subtle and prone to illusions.
This highlights the importance of deep contemplation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : إِن ترجيح الفاعل المختار عندنا لأحد مقدوريه ) على الآخر ( إنما هو بمجرد الإرادة ولا حاجة فيه ) أي في ذلك الترجيح ( إلى ) داع ( مرجح ينضم إليه كما تقدم تحقيقه في مثال طريقي الهارب من السبع وقد حي العطشان ) . فنقول : الفاعلية حادثة بمجرد الإرادة المتعلقة بالمقدور وقد يقال : هذه الإرادة المستلزمة لوجود المقدور إن كانت قديمة لزم قدم المقدور وإن كانت حادثة احتاجت إلى إرادة أخرى أو شيء آخر حادث فيلزم التسلسل. ويجاب: إما بجواز ترتب الإرادات أو ترتب تعلقات إرادة واحدة قديمة إلى ما لا يتناهى وإما بجواز حدوث تعلقها في وقت معين بلا سبب مخصص لكون التعلق أمراً اعتبارياً فعليك بالتدبر فيها والتثبت في مزال الأوهام في أمثال هذه المقامات
The second aspect: Indeed, the preponderance of the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) for one of his two potential objects over the other is only by mere will (al-irada), and there is no need in that preponderance for a motive (da'i) or preponderant (murajjih) to join it, as has been previously established in the example of the two paths of the one fleeing from a lion and the thirsty man. So we say: The agency is temporal by mere will that is related to the potential object. And it may be said: This will that entails the existence of the potential object—if it were eternal, the eternity of the potential object would follow; if it were temporal, it would need another will or something else temporal, leading to an infinite chain. And it is answered: Either by the permissibility of the succession of wills (tartub al-iradat) or the succession of relations (ta'alluqat) of a single eternal will ad infinitum, or by the permissibility of the temporal occurrence of its relation at a specific time without a specifying cause, because the relation is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari). So you must reflect carefully and be steadfast in the slips of illusions in such stations.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the second reliable answer to the doubt (likely the same doubt about the temporal origination of the world from an eternal agent). The author states that the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) preponderates one of his two potential objects (maqdur) over the other solely by mere will (irada), without needing any additional motive (da'i) or preponderant (murajjih). This has been previously explained using the example of two paths: one fleeing from a lion chooses one path by mere will, and the thirsty man chooses water. The author then asserts: the agency (fa'iliyya) is temporal, occurring by the mere will that is related (muta'alliqa) to the potential object. An objection is anticipated: This will, which entails the existence of the potential object, either is eternal (qadima) or temporal (haditha). If eternal, then the potential object would be eternal (since the effect cannot be separated from the will). If temporal, it would require another will or another temporal thing, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul). The author provides two answers: First, the permissibility of a succession of multiple temporal wills (tartub al-iradat) ad infinitum, or the succession of relations (ta'alluqat) of a single eternal will ad infinitum. Second, the permissibility of the temporal occurrence of the relation (ta'alluq) of the will to the object at a specific time without any specifying cause, because the relation is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari) and not a real entity that requires a cause. The author then exhorts the reader to reflect carefully and be steadfast, as these are subtle matters prone to illusions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The will must have a cause outside itself for it to choose one object over another.
Correction: The author argues that the free agent's preponderance is by mere will, and the relation of the will to its object is a mental consideration that can be temporally specified without a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer, what is the basis of the free agent's preponderance of one potential object over another?
A) A preponderant motive that joins the will.
B) Mere will, without need for any additional motive.
C) An external cause that determines the choice.
D) The inherent goodness of the potential object.

Key Takeaway
The free agent's preference is by mere will; the relation of will to its object is a mental consideration that can occur without a specifying cause, avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think back to a recent decision where you felt torn between two options. In that moment, did you sense a 'cause' pushing your will, or did you simply choose by your own will? According to this lesson, your will's specification has no external cause. Do you truly live as if your choices are your own, or do you often blame circumstances? What would change if you owned that responsibility fully today?
?
The author says the relation of the will to its object is a 'mental consideration' (amr i'tibari). This means the moment you decide, there is no external factor making you decide; it is purely from within. Consider a private struggle—like choosing to worship sincerely or to avoid a sin. Are you willing to see that the only thing stopping you is your own will, not lack of motivation or circumstances? How will you act on this understanding in the next hour?
?
If your will's choice is not determined by any cause, then every choice is a pure expression of your inner self. Reflect on a choice you are about to make today (big or small). Are you ready to make it with full awareness that you are completely responsible for it? What is one concrete action you can take right now to exercise your free will in a way that aligns with what you know to be true?

Fourth Doubt: Eternal Possibility and the Argument from Generosity
Presentation of the fourth doubt: the world's eternal possibility and the argument that the generous must bestow existence, with the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt concerning the world's eternal possibility and the argument from generosity, and why the response distinguishes between eternal possibility and eternal existence for a temporal thing.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility does not entail eternal existence for a temporal thing.

In this passage, the author presents a doubt raised by those who argue for the world's pre-eternity. The doubt claims that since the world's possibility is eternal, its existence must also be eternal, especially given God's generosity. However, the response shows that this argument is merely rhetorical and that a temporal thing, considered as temporal, cannot be eternal even if its possibility is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to grasp the overall flow of the doubt, the argument from generosity, and the response.
The Arabic contains a parenthetical error; 'لا) أول' should be 'لا أول'.
2
Identify the components of the doubt
Notice the doubt first states that the world's possibility is eternal, then that the possibility of God's effect is eternal, and that this invalidates the theologians' proofs. Then the argument from generosity is presented.
The doubt is built on the idea that if possibility were not eternal, it would change from impossibility to possibility, which is absurd.
3
Understand the two-part response
The author answers that the argument from generosity is merely rhetorical, not demonstrative. Then he explains that eternal possibility does not imply the possibility of eternal existence for a temporal thing, because the temporal thing is conditioned by its temporality.
This distinction hinges on taking the thing 'in itself' versus 'with the condition of being temporal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة ( الرابعة : صحة العالم أي إمكان وجوده لا) أول) لها وإلا لزم الانقلاب من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي وأنه يرفع الأمان عن البديهات كجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات ) وكذلك صحة تأثير الباري فيه أي وكذا إمكان تأثيره تعالى والعالم لا أول له وإلا لزم الانقلاب المذكور وحينئذ فيجب أن يجزم بإمكان وجود العالم ( في الأزل من الصانع ( وهو يبطل دلائلهم أي دلائل المتكلمين على امتناع وجوده فيه ( ثم) أي بعد ثبوت إمكان وجوده وصدوره أزلا نقول : ترك الجود) الذي هو إفاضة الوجود عليه زماناً غير متناه لا يليق بالجواد (المطلق) الكامل من جميع الجهات في كونه جواداً . فوجب قدم وجوده وإلا لزم تعطله . ( والجواب : إنه ) أي ما ذكرتموه من حديث الجود ولزوم التعطل كلام ( خطابي ( لا يجدي نفعا فيما نحن فيه من البرهانيات ) ثم إنه لا يلزم من أزلية الصحة صحة الأزلية كفي الحادث بشرط كونه حادثا ) فإن إمكانه أزلي لما ذكرتم وليست أزليته ممكنة لاستحالة الأزلية مع شرط الحدوث وقد عرفت أنه إذا أخذ ذات الحادث من حيث هو كان إمكانه أزليا وأمكن أزليته أيضاً وإذا أخذ بشرط الحدوث لم يكن له إمكان من هذه الحيثية فضلا عن أن يكون إمكانه أزلياً .
The fourth doubt: The validity of the world—i.e., the possibility of its existence—has no beginning; otherwise, there would be a conversion from essential impossibility to essential possibility, and that would undermine confidence in self-evident truths, such as the possibility of possible things and the impossibility of impossible things. Likewise, the validity of the effect of the Creator in it—i.e., the possibility of His effect, may He be exalted—and the world has no beginning; otherwise, the mentioned conversion would occur. And then it must be affirmed that the existence of the world from the Maker is possible in eternity, and this invalidates their proofs—i.e., the proofs of the theologians (mutakallimun) for the impossibility of its existence in eternity. Then, after establishing the possibility of its existence and its emanation eternally, we say: The abandonment of generosity (al-jud), which is the emanation (ifada) of existence upon it for a non-finite time, is not befitting of the absolute generous one (al-jawad al-mutlaq), perfect in all respects in being generous. So the eternity of its existence is necessary; otherwise, there would be idleness (ta'til). The answer: Indeed, what you have mentioned about generosity and the necessity of idleness is rhetorical speech (kalam khitabi) that is of no benefit in what we are dealing with, which is demonstrative matters (burhaniyat). Moreover, from the eternality of validity (azaliyyat al-sihha) does not follow the validity of eternality (sihhat al-azaliyya), as in the case of the temporal event conditioned by being temporal. For its possibility is eternal as you mentioned, but its eternality is not possible because of the impossibility of eternality together with the condition of temporality. And you have known that when the essence of the temporal event is taken in itself (min haythu huwa), its possibility is eternal and its eternality is also possible; but when it is taken with the condition of temporality (bi-shart al-huduth), it has no possibility from this respect, let alone its possibility being eternal.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt (shubha) concerning the temporal origination of the world. The doubt is: The world's possibility (sihha or imkan) of existence has no beginning (la awwal laha). That is, the world was always possible to exist from all eternity. If it were not always possible, there would be a change from essential impossibility (imtina' dhati) to essential possibility (imkan dhati), which would undermine self-evident truths (badahat). Similarly, the possibility of the Creator's effect upon the world is also eternal. Therefore, one must affirm that the world's existence from the Maker is possible in eternity, which invalidates the proofs of the theologians (mutakallimun) that the world cannot exist in eternity. Then a further point is made: After establishing the possibility of the world's existence and its emanation (sudur) eternally, the opponent argues that the generous (jawad) must bestow existence, otherwise there would be idleness (ta'til), and so the world must be eternal. The author replies: This argument from generosity is merely rhetorical (kalam khitabi) and does not serve in demonstrative contexts (burhaniyat). Moreover, the eternality of possibility (azaliyyat al-sihha) does not entail the possibility of eternal existence (sihhat al-azaliyya). For a temporal event (hadith) that is conditioned by being temporal (bi-shart al-huduth) cannot be eternal. In itself (min haythu huwa), considered without condition, its possibility is eternal, and even its eternal existence is possible. But when taken with the condition of temporality, it cannot be eternal. Therefore, the possibility being eternal does not force the world to be eternal.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that if something is eternally possible, it must be able to exist eternally.
Correction: For a temporal event, eternal possibility does not force eternal existence because the event is conditioned by temporality; in itself it could be eternal, but with the condition it cannot.

Checkpoint

A) The difference between essential and acquired possibility.
B) The distinction between taking a temporal event in itself and taking it with the condition of temporality.
C) The difference between potential and actual existence.
D) The distinction between God's will and His power.

Key Takeaway
The fourth doubt is refuted by showing that the argument from generosity is rhetorical, and that eternal possibility does not force eternal existence when the temporal event is considered with its condition of temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
