?
The author distinguishes between taking a thing 'in itself' and 'with a condition'. In your own life, are there assumptions you hold about yourself or God that confuse these two perspectives? What would change if you applied this distinction to a personal belief you have?

The Possibility of the World's Annihilation
Introduction to the second aim: discussing whether the world can cease to exist after coming into being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of the world's annihilation is a direct consequence of its temporal origination (huduth).

Core Idea
The possibility of the world’s annihilation depends on whether it is eternal or temporally originated.

This lesson introduces the second major aim of the observatory: the possibility of the annihilation of the world after its existence. The author shows that this question is a branch of the debate on temporal origination. By distinguishing the positions of those who hold the world as eternal versus those who hold it as temporally originated, the author sets the stage for deeper arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the distinction. Note that the author frames the question as a branch of huduth (temporal origination).
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the eternalist position (denies annihilation) and the temporalist position (affirms annihilation).
3
Understand the reasoning
For eternalists, the eternal cannot cease; for temporalists, the essence in itself is receptive to nonexistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثاني : في صحة فناء العالم ] ( في صحة فناء العالم بعد وجوده ) وهو فرع الحدوث فمن قال: إنه قديم قال : لا يجوز عدمه لما تقدم في بيان حدوث السكون من أن القديم لا يجوز عدمه ) وأما من قال : إنه حادث فقد قال بجواز فنائه لكون ماهيته من حيث هي قابله للعدم )
The second aim: On the validity of the annihilation of the world. (On the validity of the annihilation of the world after its existence.) And it is a branch of temporal origination. So whoever said that it is eternal said: Its non-existence is not permissible, because of what preceded in the explanation of the temporality of rest (sukun), that the eternal is not permissible to cease. And as for whoever said that it is temporal, he said that its annihilation is permissible, because its essence (mahiyya) in itself (min haythu hiya) is receptive to non-existence.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage introduces the second aim (al-maqsad al-thani) of the observatory (al-marsad). The topic is the validity (sihha) of the annihilation (fana') of the world after its existence. The author states that this question is a branch (far') of the question of temporal origination (huduth). He presents two positions: First, those who say the world is eternal (qadim) hold that its non-existence (adam) is not permissible (la yajuz 'adamuhu), because it has been established earlier (in the context of the temporality of rest, sukun) that what is eternal cannot cease to exist. Second, those who say the world is temporal (hadith) hold that its annihilation is permissible (yajuz fana'uhu), because its essence (mahiyya) considered in itself (min haythu hiya) is receptive (qabila) to non-existence. This sets the stage for further arguments on the topic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that if the world currently exists, it cannot cease to exist because existence is permanent.
Correction: The author explains that for those who affirm temporal origination, the world's essence in itself is receptive to non-existence, so annihilation is possible despite its current existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do those who affirm the world's temporal origination (huduth) also affirm the possibility of its annihilation (fana')?
A) Because the world is eternal and cannot cease.
B) Because the world's essence considered in itself is receptive to non-existence.
C) Because God's power is limited.
D) Because the world is composed of atoms that naturally dissolve.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the world's annihilation hinges on whether it is eternal or temporally originated; if temporally originated, its essence is receptive to non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the three things you cling to most in this world: a possession, a relationship, an achievement. If you truly believed the world could vanish in the next moment, would your attachment to them change? Why or why not? Let this question unsettle you until you find an honest answer.
?
The passage states that the world's essence is 'receptive to non-existence.' Does your daily life reflect that reality? Or do you act as if this world is permanent? Before you sleep tonight, consciously rehearse: 'This world can cease to exist; my soul is the only thing that lasts.' Commit to that thought.

The Fourth Doubt: The Argument for Eternal Possibility
al-Iji presents the fourth doubt and its two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt that the possibility of the world must be eternal and the argument that divine generosity requires the world to be eternal.

Core Idea
Even if possibility is eternal, actual eternity is impossible for created things.

This lesson introduces the fourth doubt raised against the theologians' position that the world is temporally originated. The doubt argues that if the possibility of the world were not eternal, a conversion from impossibility to possibility would occur, which is absurd. Furthermore, it claims that divine generosity demands that God create the world eternally, otherwise He would be withholding goodness. We will see the structure of these arguments before moving to the response in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the flow of the doubt.
The doubt consists of two main arguments.
2
Identify the two prongs
Find the conversion argument (first part) and the generosity argument (second part). Understand how each tries to prove the world must be eternal.
3
Understand the conversion argument
The doubt claims that if possibility were not eternal, it would change from impossible to possible, undermining axioms. Reflect on why this is considered a problem.
4
Understand the generosity argument
The doubt argues that a perfectly generous God would not delay granting existence for infinite time, so the world must be eternal. Note the rhetorical tone.
This argument is later dismissed as rhetorical.
5
Prepare for the response
After understanding the doubt, anticipate how a theologian might respond. The next lesson will give the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة: صحة العالم أي إمكان وجوده لا أول لها وإلا لزم الانقلاب من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي وأنه يرفع الأمان عن البديهات كجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات وكذلك صحة تأثير الباري فيه أي وكذا إمكان تأثيره تعالى والعالم لا أول له وإلا لزم الانقلاب المذكور وحينئذ فيجب أن يجزم بإمكان وجود العالم في الأزل من الصانع وهو يبطل دلائلهم أي دلائل المتكلمين على امتناع وجوده فيه ثم أي بعد ثبوت إمكان وجوده وصدوره أزلا نقول: ترك الجود الذي هو إفاضة الوجود عليه زماناً غير متناه لا يليق بالجواد المطلق الكامل من جميع الجهات في كونه جواداً فوجب قدم وجوده وإلا لزم تعطله. والجواب: إنه أي ما ذكرتموه من حديث الجود ولزوم التعطل كلام خطابي لا يجدي نفعا فيما نحن فيه من البرهانيات ثم إنه لا يلزم من أزلية الصحة صحة الأزلية كفي الحادث بشرط كونه حادثا فإن إمكانه أزلي لما ذكرتم وليست أزليته ممكنة لاستحالة الأزلية مع شرط الحدوث وقد عرفت أنه إذا أخذ ذات الحادث من حيث هو كان إمكانه أزليا وأمكن أزليته أيضاً وإذا أخذ بشرط الحدوث لم يكن له إمكان من هذه الحيثية فضلا عن أن يكون إمكانه أزلياً.
The Fourth Doubt: The soundness (ṣiḥḥa) of the world, i.e., the possibility of its existence having no beginning—otherwise a conversion from intrinsic impossibility to intrinsic possibility would follow, and that would undermine confidence in axioms, such as the possibility of possibles and the impossibility of impossibles. And likewise the soundness of the effect of the Creator in it, i.e., the possibility of His effect—may He be exalted—and the world having no beginning—otherwise the aforementioned conversion would follow. Then it is necessary to be certain of the possibility of the world’s existence in pre-eternity from the Maker, and that invalidates their proofs, i.e., the proofs of the theologians for the impossibility of its existence therein. Then, after establishing the possibility of its existence and emanation from Him pre-eternally, we say: Abandoning generosity, which is the effusion of existence upon it for an infinite time, is not befitting the absolute generous one, perfect in all respects in being generous. So the pre-eternity of its existence is necessary, otherwise its suspension (ta‘aṭṭul) would follow. And the answer: Indeed, what you have mentioned about generosity and the necessity of suspension is rhetorical speech (khiṭābī), of no benefit in what we are dealing with concerning demonstrative matters. Moreover, the pre-eternity of possibility does not entail the possibility of pre-eternality, as in the case of the originated thing with the condition of its being originated: its possibility is pre-eternal for what you have mentioned, but its pre-eternality is not possible due to the impossibility of pre-eternality together with the condition of temporal origination. And you have known that when the essence of the originated is taken absolutely, its possibility is pre-eternal and its pre-eternality is also possible; but when taken with the condition of temporal origination, it has no possibility from this aspect, let alone that its possibility be pre-eternal.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt regarding the possibility of the world being eternal (having no beginning). The doubt argues that if the possibility of the world’s existence were not eternal, then conversion from intrinsic impossibility to possibility would occur, undermining axioms. Also, the possibility of God’s effect being eternal is argued similarly. Then a second argument from generosity is presented: it is incompatible with God’s generosity to withhold existence for infinite time, so the world must be eternal. The answer first dismisses the generosity argument as rhetorical, not demonstrative. Then the key response: pre-eternity of possibility does not imply possibility of pre-eternality. For an originated thing, when considered with the condition of its being originated, pre-eternality is impossible even though its possibility considered absolutely might be pre-eternal. The author ends by clarifying the nuance: if we take the essence of the originated without condition, its possibility is pre-eternal and its pre-eternality is also possible; but when conditioned by temporal origination, it has no possibility from that aspect.

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Doubt: The Distinction of Conditions
al-Iji provides the refutation of the fourth doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the pre-eternity of possibility does not entail the possibility of pre-eternality for originated things, and how the answer distinguishes between absolute and conditioned possibility.

Core Idea
An originated thing considered as originated cannot be pre-eternal, even if its possibility considered absolutely is pre-eternal.

In this lesson, we examine the theologian's response to the fourth doubt. First, the argument from generosity is dismissed as rhetorical, not demonstrative. Then the key philosophical distinction is made: the pre-eternity of possibility does not imply pre-eternity of the possible thing when that thing is conditioned by temporal origination. This nuanced distinction resolves the doubt without undermining the theologians' proofs for the temporal origination of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: first dismissal, then the main distinction.
2
Understand the dismissal of the generosity argument
The answer calls the generosity argument 'rhetorical speech' (khiṭābī) and says it has no place in demonstrative reasoning. This shows the need for logical rigor.
This does not deny God's generosity but notes it is not a proof for the world's eternity.
3
Grasp the key distinction
The response explains that pre-eternity of possibility does not entail possibility of pre-eternality. It uses the example of an originated thing: its possibility is pre-eternal, but its pre-eternality is impossible because it is conditioned by temporal origination.
The condition (sharṭ) of being originated prevents pre-eternality.
4
Apply the distinction to the doubt
The doubt mistakenly assumed that eternal possibility implies eternal actuality. The response shows that when we consider the thing as originated (bi-sharṭ kawnihi ḥādithan), its pre-eternality is impossible, even if its possibility considered absolutely is eternal.
This resolves the doubt without contradicting axioms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة: صحة العالم أي إمكان وجوده لا أول لها وإلا لزم الانقلاب من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي وأنه يرفع الأمان عن البديهات كجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات وكذلك صحة تأثير الباري فيه أي وكذا إمكان تأثيره تعالى والعالم لا أول له وإلا لزم الانقلاب المذكور وحينئذ فيجب أن يجزم بإمكان وجود العالم في الأزل من الصانع وهو يبطل دلائلهم أي دلائل المتكلمين على امتناع وجوده فيه ثم أي بعد ثبوت إمكان وجوده وصدوره أزلا نقول: ترك الجود الذي هو إفاضة الوجود عليه زماناً غير متناه لا يليق بالجواد المطلق الكامل من جميع الجهات في كونه جواداً فوجب قدم وجوده وإلا لزم تعطله. والجواب: إنه أي ما ذكرتموه من حديث الجود ولزوم التعطل كلام خطابي لا يجدي نفعا فيما نحن فيه من البرهانيات ثم إنه لا يلزم من أزلية الصحة صحة الأزلية كفي الحادث بشرط كونه حادثا فإن إمكانه أزلي لما ذكرتم وليست أزليته ممكنة لاستحالة الأزلية مع شرط الحدوث وقد عرفت أنه إذا أخذ ذات الحادث من حيث هو كان إمكانه أزليا وأمكن أزليته أيضاً وإذا أخذ بشرط الحدوث لم يكن له إمكان من هذه الحيثية فضلا عن أن يكون إمكانه أزلياً.
The Fourth Doubt: The soundness (ṣiḥḥa) of the world, i.e., the possibility of its existence having no beginning—otherwise a conversion from intrinsic impossibility to intrinsic possibility would follow, and that would undermine confidence in axioms, such as the possibility of possibles and the impossibility of impossibles. And likewise the soundness of the effect of the Creator in it, i.e., the possibility of His effect—may He be exalted—and the world having no beginning—otherwise the aforementioned conversion would follow. Then it is necessary to be certain of the possibility of the world’s existence in pre-eternity from the Maker, and that invalidates their proofs, i.e., the proofs of the theologians for the impossibility of its existence therein. Then, after establishing the possibility of its existence and emanation from Him pre-eternally, we say: Abandoning generosity, which is the effusion of existence upon it for an infinite time, is not befitting the absolute generous one, perfect in all respects in being generous. So the pre-eternity of its existence is necessary, otherwise its suspension (ta‘aṭṭul) would follow. And the answer: Indeed, what you have mentioned about generosity and the necessity of suspension is rhetorical speech (khiṭābī), of no benefit in what we are dealing with concerning demonstrative matters. Moreover, the pre-eternity of possibility does not entail the possibility of pre-eternality, as in the case of the originated thing with the condition of its being originated: its possibility is pre-eternal for what you have mentioned, but its pre-eternality is not possible due to the impossibility of pre-eternality together with the condition of temporal origination. And you have known that when the essence of the originated is taken absolutely, its possibility is pre-eternal and its pre-eternality is also possible; but when taken with the condition of temporal origination, it has no possibility from this aspect, let alone that its possibility be pre-eternal.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt regarding the possibility of the world being eternal (having no beginning). The doubt argues that if the possibility of the world’s existence were not eternal, then conversion from intrinsic impossibility to possibility would occur, undermining axioms. Also, the possibility of God’s effect being eternal is argued similarly. Then a second argument from generosity is presented: it is incompatible with God’s generosity to withhold existence for infinite time, so the world must be eternal. The answer first dismisses the generosity argument as rhetorical, not demonstrative. Then the key response: pre-eternity of possibility does not imply possibility of pre-eternality. For an originated thing, when considered with the condition of its being originated, pre-eternality is impossible even though its possibility considered absolutely might be pre-eternal. The author ends by clarifying the nuance: if we take the essence of the originated without condition, its possibility is pre-eternal and its pre-eternality is also possible; but when conditioned by temporal origination, it has no possibility from that aspect.

شرط الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is possible in pre-eternity, it can be eternal.
Correction: The possibility of a thing being pre-eternal depends on its nature. An originated thing, by virtue of being originated, cannot be pre-eternal even if its possibility considered absolutely is eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the pre-eternity of possibility not entail the possibility of pre-eternality for an originated thing?
A) Because possibility is itself eternal
B) Because of the condition of temporal origination
C) Because God's generosity is not relevant
D) Because the doubt is rhetorical

Key Takeaway
The pre-eternity of possibility does not guarantee possibility of pre-eternality, especially when the thing is conditioned as temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence as a contingent being, do you tend to think of yourself in absolute terms or as conditioned by time and change? How does the distinction between these two perspectives affect your understanding of your dependence on God?
?
The argument dismisses the generosity objection as rhetorical, not demonstrative. Are there areas in your faith where you rely more on emotional or comforting arguments than on solid evidence? Will you commit to seeking demonstrative proofs for your beliefs, even when they are challenging?
?
If the possibility of your faith was pre-eternal in God's knowledge, but your actual faith is temporally originated and conditioned by your choices, what does that imply about your responsibility to act now? Are you living as if your faith is absolute or actually conditioning it by your daily actions?

The Possibility of the Annihilation of the World
Al-Iji explains the rational possibility of the world's annihilation and discusses the Karramiyya's opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of the world's annihilation follows from its temporal origination, and why the Karramiyya uniquely deny it.

Core Idea
If the world is originated, its annihilation is possible; only the Karramiyya say otherwise.

Al-Iji begins by linking annihilation to the world's origin. He argues that once we accept the world is temporally originated, its future annihilation is rationally possible. He then contrasts the universal agreement on possibility with the Karramiyya's contrary stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Iji uses a very concise style with references to previous discussions.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify the key argument: non-existence before existence and after existence are alike in kind, so if the world was preceded by non-existence, it can also be followed by non-existence.
This argument establishes the rational possibility, not actual occurrence.
3
Examine the Karramiyya position
Note that the Karramiyya accept temporal origination of bodies but deny their annihilation. They apply the same reasoning used to deny the permanence of accidents to bodies themselves.
Al-Iji dismisses their proof by referring to an earlier refutation without repeating it.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this topic matters: the possibility of annihilation has implications for eschatology and divine power. The unanimous agreement of Ahl al-Sunna on this point is highlighted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في صحة فناء العالم بعد وجوده وهو فرع الحدوث فمن قال: إنه قديم قال: لا يجوز عدمه لما تقدم في بيان حدوث السكون من أن القديم لا يجوز عدمه وأما من قال: إنه حادث فقد قال بجواز فنائه لكون ماهيته من حيث هي قابلة للعدم حيث كانت متصفة به والعدم قبل أي قبل الوجود كالعدم بعد أي بعده لا تمايز بينهما ولا اختلاف فيهما فما جاز عليه أحدهما جاز عليه الآخر فقد ثبت جواز الفناء وأما وقوعه فقد توقف فيه بعضهم، وأول الآيات الدالة عليه لم يخالف في ذلك أحد إلا الكرامية فإنهم مع اعترافهم بحدوث الأجسام قالوا: إنها أبدية ممتنع فناؤها، ودليلهم على ذلك ما أشرنا إليه في امتناع بقاء الأعراض، والكرامية طروده في الأجسام فقالوا: لو عدم الجسم بعد بقائه لكان عدمه إما لذاته وإما لامر آخر وجودي أو عدمي إلى آخر ما مر هناك، والكل باطل فلا يصح عدمه فالتفت إليه مع جوابه المذكور هناك محضراً عندك فلا حاجة إلى إعادتهما.
On the soundness (ṣiḥḥa) of the annihilation of the world after its existence. And it is a branch of temporal origination. Whoever says it is eternal says: Its non-existence is not permissible, as previously established in the exposition of the origination of rest, that the eternal is not permissible to cease to exist. As for whoever says it is originated, he says that its annihilation is permissible because its quiddity (māhiyya) insofar as it is receptive of non-existence, given that it is qualified by it (i.e., non-existence). And non-existence before, i.e., before existence, is like non-existence after, i.e., after it—there is no distinction between them, nor any difference. So what is permissible for one is permissible for the other. Thus, the permissibility of annihilation is established. As for its actual occurrence, some have hesitated, and they interpreted the verses indicating it. No one disagreed on that except the Karramiyya, for they, while acknowledging the temporal origination of bodies, said they are eternal and their annihilation is impossible. Their proof for that is what we referred to about the impossibility of the permanence of accidents, and the Karramiyya applied it to bodies. They said: If a body were to cease to exist after its permanence, its non-existence would be either due to itself, or due to another thing, whether existent or non-existent, etc., as was discussed there. And all of that is false, so its non-existence is not sound. So refer to it along with its answer mentioned there, present with you, so there is no need to repeat them.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage begins the second purpose: the possibility of the world's annihilation after existence. It is a branch of the world's temporal origination. Those who hold the world eternal deny the possibility of its annihilation, based on the principle that the eternal cannot cease to exist. Those who hold it originated affirm its possibility because its quiddity is receptive of non-existence. The argument: non-existence before existence and after existence are indistinguishable, so if non-existence before existence was possible, then non-existence after is also possible. Thus annihilation is possible. As for its actual occurrence, some hesitated and interpreted scriptural evidence. Only the Karramiyya hold that bodies are eternal and cannot be annihilated, despite accepting temporal origination. Their proof is the same as their proof for the impossibility of the permanence of accidents: if a body were to cease, its non-existence would have to be due to itself or something else, all false. The author says the answer to that has been given previously, so it is not repeated.

حدوث
قديم
ماهية
عدم
كرامية
أبدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Karramiyya deny temporal origination of the world.
Correction: The text explicitly states they acknowledge temporal origination of bodies (حدوث الأجسام), but they claim bodies are eternal (أبدية) in the sense of everlasting, so annihilation is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why do the Karramiyya deny the possibility of the annihilation of bodies?
A) Because bodies are eternal in the sense of having no beginning
B) Because they deny temporal origination of the world
C) Because they apply the same reasoning as for the impossibility of the permanence of accidents
D) Because they interpret scriptural verses differently

Key Takeaway
The world's annihilation is rationally possible because its quiddity is receptive of non-existence; the Karramiyya's objection is a misapplication of a previous argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world could be annihilated at any moment, what would you regret not having done for the sake of Allah? Why are you not doing that today?
?
The Karramiyya relied on a flawed argument to assert the world's permanence. What false sense of permanence do you cling to in your daily life that makes you forget the transient nature of this world, and how can you break that attachment?
?
The author argues that non-existence before and after are identical. If your own existence is just as fragile as a world that could cease, how does that change what you prioritize between now and your death?

Bodies Are Permanent: The Rational Necessity Argument
Al-Amidi's argument from rational necessity and the objection-sensation response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational necessity argument for the permanence of bodies and the Ash'ari response to the objection that sensation is unreliable.

Core Idea
Rational necessity, not sensation, proves the permanence of bodies.

This lesson explores the central proof for bodily permanence: al-Amidi's argument from rational necessity. We witness the same mountains and persons persist, and this knowledge is not sensory but innate rational certainty. However, an objection arises: sensation seems to give this knowledge, and it misleads about accidents. The Ash'ari response clarifies that genuine certainty is from rational self-evidence, requiring no external support.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Notice the main sections: statement of doctrine, al-Amidi's argument, the objection, and the response.
2
Identify al-Amidi's argument
Find the sentence where al-Amidi says 'we know by rational necessity that...' and understand the examples.
The argument is a direct appeal to self-evident knowledge.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that our certainty comes from sensation, which is unreliable because it also suggests accidents persist.
This is a strong objection that the Ash'ari must answer.
4
Study the response
The response asserts that the certainty is from rational necessity, not sensation. The self-evident needs no proof; it is a judgment of innate disposition.
This defines the meaning of 'badihi' (self-evident).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجسام باقية خلافاً للنظام فإنه ذهب إلى أنها متجددة آنا فاناً كالأعراض. وقيل: هذا النقل عنه غير معتمد عليه لأنه قال باحتياج الأجسام إلى المؤثر حال البقاء فتوهمت النقلة أنه لا يقول ببقائها ومن أصحابنا أي ومن الأشاعرة من ادعى فيه الضرورة أي البداهة قال الآمدي: نحن نعلم بالضرورة العقلية أن ما شاهدناه بالأمس من الجبال الراسيات والأرضين والسماوات هو عين ما نشاهده اليوم وكذا نعلم بالاضطرار أن من فاتحناه بالكلام هو عين من ختمناه معه. وإن أولادنا ورفقاءنا الآن الذين كانوا معنا من قبل لا يقال: ليس ذلك أي جزمنا ببقائها ضرورة إلا لبقائها في الحس فإنه يشهد باستمرار الأجسام ولا يصلح الحس وشهادته بالبقاء للتعويل عليه والوثوق به إذ الأعراض كذلك لأن الحسن شاهد ببقائها. وقد قلتم أيها الأشاعرة بأنها لا تبقى زمانين بل هناك أمثال متجددة لم يدرك الحس تفاوتها فحسبها أمراً واحداً مستمراً فكيف تقبلون شهادته في الأجسام دون الأعراض؟ قلنا: أي لأنا نقول: لا نسلم أن ذلك الجزم منا ليس إلا للبقاء في الحس حتى يتجه عليه ما ذكرتموه بل الضرورة العقلية حاصلة بلا شبهة والضروري البديهي لا يطلب مستنده بل هو ما يجزم به مجرد الفطرة عند تصور الطرفين وملاحظة النسبة فإن ذلك هو معنى البديهي المرادف للأولى ومنهم من استدل عليه بأنه لو لم تكن الأجسام باقية لارتفع الموت والحياة أي لم يكن أن يقال لموت حي أو حياة ميت لأن محلهما يجب أن يكون واحداً وعلى ذلك التقدير فالجسم حال حياته غير الجسم حال مماته فلا يكونان واردين على موضوع واحد ولارتفع التسخن والتبرد والتسود والتبيض ونظائرها، أي لم يكن القول بالاستحالة أصلا بأنها مشروطة باتحاد المحل وكل ذلك باطل بالضرورة العقلية.
Bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam, for he held that they are renewed moment by moment like accidents. And it is said: This transmission from him is not reliable, because he said that bodies need an effector during permanence, so the transmitters assumed that he does not affirm their permanence. And among our companions, i.e., among the Ash‘aris, there are those who claimed necessity (ḍarūra) in it, i.e., self-evidence (badāha). Al-Amidi said: We know by rational necessity that what we saw yesterday of lofty mountains, earths, and heavens is the very same as what we see today. And likewise we know by necessity that the person we began speaking to is the same as the person we finished speaking with. And that our children and companions now who were with us before—it cannot be said that that, i.e., our certainty of their permanence, is only due to their permanence in sensation, for sensation testifies to the continuity of bodies but sensation and its testimony to permanence are not reliable to rely upon, since accidents are such—for sensation testified to their permanence. And you, O Ash‘aris, have said that they do not persist for two moments, but rather there are renewed like acts (amthāl) that sensation does not perceive their difference, so it considered them as one continuous thing. So how can you accept its testimony for bodies but not for accidents? We say: Because we say we do not concede that that certainty of ours is only due to permanence in sensation, such that what you mentioned would be directed against it. Rather, rational necessity is present without doubt. And the necessary (ḍarūrī) (self-evident) does not seek a basis; rather, it is what the mere innate disposition (fiṭra) asserts when the two ends are conceived and the relation is observed. For that is the meaning of the self-evident (badīhī) synonymous with the primary (awwalī). And among them are those who argued for it by saying: If bodies were not permanent, then death and life would be impossible, i.e., it could not be said that a living being dies or a dead person lives, because their substrate must be one, and on that assumption the body during life is different from the body during death, so they would not be occurring on a single subject. And heating and cooling, blackening and whitening, and their likes would be impossible, i.e., the statement about transformation would have no basis because it is conditioned on the unity of the subject. And all of that is false by rational necessity.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is the third section: bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam who held they are renewed moment by moment like accidents. Some say this report about al-Nazzam is unreliable because he actually said bodies need an effector during permanence, which was misinterpreted. The author then presents the evidence. First, from al-Amidi: we know by rational necessity that mountains etc. yesterday are the same as today, and that persons persist. An objection is raised: this certainty might come from sensation testifying to continuity, but sensation is unreliable because it also testifies to the permanence of accidents, which Ash‘aris deny are permanent. So how can you accept sensation for bodies but not for accidents? The answer: the certainty is not from sensation but from rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya). The necessary (ḍarūrī) is self-evident and needs no proof; it is a judgment of innate disposition when the terms and relation are conceived. Then another argument: if bodies were not permanent, then change of life/death, heating/cooling etc. would be impossible because these require a single persisting subject. Since we know these changes occur, bodies must be permanent.

Key Terms
أجسام باقية
نظام
ضرورة عقلية
بداهة
أعراض
آناً فآنًا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Our certainty about the permanence of bodies comes from sensory experience.
Correction: The text argues that it comes from rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya), not from sensation, because sensation is unreliable and gave false evidence about accidents.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi's argument, how do we know bodies are permanent?
A) From sensory observation
B) From rational necessity
C) From scriptural evidence
D) From the consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The permanence of bodies is known by rational self-evidence, not by sensory testimony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that you know by rational necessity that the mountains you saw yesterday are the same today. But what about the spiritual realities Allah has shown you—do you trust them with the same certainty, or do you let your doubts and fleeting feelings erode that knowledge? Where is your fiṭra being overridden by your nafs? Commit today to act on what you rationally know to be true about Allah's permanence, even when your senses or emotions waver.
?
Al-Amidi says the self-evident does not seek a basis. Is there a core truth of your faith—like Allah's existence, His mercy, or the Hereafter—that you keep seeking 'proofs' for instead of surrendering to the innate certainty already placed in your heart? What would change if you simply trusted that fiṭra and stopped demanding extra evidence? Write one area where you will stop overanalyzing and start submitting.

Bodies Are Permanent: The Argument from Change
Conditional proof from the necessity of a permanent subject for change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional proof that the possibility of change—such as death and life—requires a permanent body.

Core Idea
Without permanent bodies, change—like death and life—would be impossible.

This lesson presents a logical argument: if bodies were not permanent, then death and life could not occur because they require a single, persisting subject. The same applies to heating, cooling, and other transformations. Since these changes are real, bodies must persist. This proof reinforces the rational necessity of bodily permanence from the nature of change.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the conditional statement: 'If bodies were not permanent, then death and life would be impossible.'
2
Understand the reasoning
The reason is that both death and life require the same substrate (subject). If the body is different at each moment, they cannot occur.
This is a logical necessity: a change presupposes something that persists.
3
Consider other examples
The same applies to heating, cooling, blackening, whitening. All require a single persisting subject.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since these changes are known to occur, the premise (bodies not permanent) must be false. Therefore, bodies are permanent.
This is a conditional proof with a modus tollens structure.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجسام باقية خلافاً للنظام فإنه ذهب إلى أنها متجددة آنا فاناً كالأعراض. وقيل: هذا النقل عنه غير معتمد عليه لأنه قال باحتياج الأجسام إلى المؤثر حال البقاء فتوهمت النقلة أنه لا يقول ببقائها ومن أصحابنا أي ومن الأشاعرة من ادعى فيه الضرورة أي البداهة قال الآمدي: نحن نعلم بالضرورة العقلية أن ما شاهدناه بالأمس من الجبال الراسيات والأرضين والسماوات هو عين ما نشاهده اليوم وكذا نعلم بالاضطرار أن من فاتحناه بالكلام هو عين من ختمناه معه. وإن أولادنا ورفقاءنا الآن الذين كانوا معنا من قبل لا يقال: ليس ذلك أي جزمنا ببقائها ضرورة إلا لبقائها في الحس فإنه يشهد باستمرار الأجسام ولا يصلح الحس وشهادته بالبقاء للتعويل عليه والوثوق به إذ الأعراض كذلك لأن الحسن شاهد ببقائها. وقد قلتم أيها الأشاعرة بأنها لا تبقى زمانين بل هناك أمثال متجددة لم يدرك الحس تفاوتها فحسبها أمراً واحداً مستمراً فكيف تقبلون شهادته في الأجسام دون الأعراض؟ قلنا: أي لأنا نقول: لا نسلم أن ذلك الجزم منا ليس إلا للبقاء في الحس حتى يتجه عليه ما ذكرتموه بل الضرورة العقلية حاصلة بلا شبهة والضروري البديهي لا يطلب مستنده بل هو ما يجزم به مجرد الفطرة عند تصور الطرفين وملاحظة النسبة فإن ذلك هو معنى البديهي المرادف للأولى ومنهم من استدل عليه بأنه لو لم تكن الأجسام باقية لارتفع الموت والحياة أي لم يكن أن يقال لموت حي أو حياة ميت لأن محلهما يجب أن يكون واحداً وعلى ذلك التقدير فالجسم حال حياته غير الجسم حال مماته فلا يكونان واردين على موضوع واحد ولارتفع التسخن والتبرد والتسود والتبيض ونظائرها، أي لم يكن القول بالاستحالة أصلا بأنها مشروطة باتحاد المحل وكل ذلك باطل بالضرورة العقلية.
Bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam, for he held that they are renewed moment by moment like accidents. And it is said: This transmission from him is not reliable, because he said that bodies need an effector during permanence, so the transmitters assumed that he does not affirm their permanence. And among our companions, i.e., among the Ash‘aris, there are those who claimed necessity (ḍarūra) in it, i.e., self-evidence (badāha). Al-Amidi said: We know by rational necessity that what we saw yesterday of lofty mountains, earths, and heavens is the very same as what we see today. And likewise we know by necessity that the person we began speaking to is the same as the person we finished speaking with. And that our children and companions now who were with us before—it cannot be said that that, i.e., our certainty of their permanence, is only due to their permanence in sensation, for sensation testifies to the continuity of bodies but sensation and its testimony to permanence are not reliable to rely upon, since accidents are such—for sensation testified to their permanence. And you, O Ash‘aris, have said that they do not persist for two moments, but rather there are renewed like acts (amthāl) that sensation does not perceive their difference, so it considered them as one continuous thing. So how can you accept its testimony for bodies but not for accidents? We say: Because we say we do not concede that that certainty of ours is only due to permanence in sensation, such that what you mentioned would be directed against it. Rather, rational necessity is present without doubt. And the necessary (ḍarūrī) (self-evident) does not seek a basis; rather, it is what the mere innate disposition (fiṭra) asserts when the two ends are conceived and the relation is observed. For that is the meaning of the self-evident (badīhī) synonymous with the primary (awwalī). And among them are those who argued for it by saying: If bodies were not permanent, then death and life would be impossible, i.e., it could not be said that a living being dies or a dead person lives, because their substrate must be one, and on that assumption the body during life is different from the body during death, so they would not be occurring on a single subject. And heating and cooling, blackening and whitening, and their likes would be impossible, i.e., the statement about transformation would have no basis because it is conditioned on the unity of the subject. And all of that is false by rational necessity.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is the third section: bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam who held they are renewed moment by moment like accidents. Some say this report about al-Nazzam is unreliable because he actually said bodies need an effector during permanence, which was misinterpreted. The author then presents the evidence. First, from al-Amidi: we know by rational necessity that mountains etc. yesterday are the same as today, and that persons persist. An objection is raised: this certainty might come from sensation testifying to continuity, but sensation is unreliable because it also testifies to the permanence of accidents, which Ash‘aris deny are permanent. So how can you accept sensation for bodies but not for accidents? The answer: the certainty is not from sensation but from rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya). The necessary (ḍarūrī) is self-evident and needs no proof; it is a judgment of innate disposition when the terms and relation are conceived. Then another argument: if bodies were not permanent, then change of life/death, heating/cooling etc. would be impossible because these require a single persisting subject. Since we know these changes occur, bodies must be permanent.

Key Terms
محل
استحالة
أجسام باقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies can change while being entirely new at each moment, with no persisting substance.
Correction: The argument shows that change requires a single persisting subject; otherwise, successive unrelated events would not be changes of the same thing.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from change, why would death and life be impossible if bodies were not permanent?
A) Because death and life are accidental and require a substrate that persists.
B) Because death and life cannot occur at the same time.
C) Because the soul would leave the body.
D) Because bodies would be annihilated and recreated differently.

Key Takeaway
The reality of change, such as life-to-death, proves that bodies persist as a single subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body undergoes constant changes—growth, illness, aging—yet you remain the same person. How does the fact that your body is a permanent substrate from Allah affect your responsibility for how you treat it? Will you change anything today knowing your body will be resurrected?
?
When you witness someone die, you see a change that requires a single subject. Does this reminder of Allah's power and the permanence of the body make you consider that your own death is approaching? What one thing can you do today to prepare for that moment?
?
The argument shows that without a permanent body, death and life would be meaningless. How does this deepen your trust in Allah's wisdom in creating a world that is both changing and permanent? What perspective does that give you on your current trials?

Al-Nazzam's Argument and the Problem of Accident Permanence
Al-Iji presents al-Nazzam's argument and the problem of circularity for accident permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's argument against bodily permanence and why it leads to circularity for accidents.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be permanent, but bodies can — the key is in the conditions of permanence.

Al-Nazzam argued that bodies cannot be permanent because accidents cannot be permanent. This lesson examines his argument, its basis in the composition of substances from accidents, and the circularity that arises if we try to make accidents permanent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nazzam applies the accident proof to bodies via analogy.
2
Understand the analogy
Al-Nazzam based his argument on the principle that substances are composed of accidents. This led him to treat bodies like accidents regarding permanence.
Al-Amidi explains that we perceive differences in bodies as we do in accidents.
3
Recognize the circularity
The text shows that if accidents were permanent, their non-existence would require absence of condition, but that condition (substance) itself depends on accidents, leading to circularity. Hence accidents cannot be permanent.
This circularity does not apply to substances, as shown in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجة النظام أنها لو بقيت لامتنع عدمها بالدليل الذي ذكرناه لبقاء الأعراض ) أي في امتناع عدمها على تقدير بقائها ( واللازم باطل اتفاقاً تنبيه : ) على منشأ مذاهب النظام والكرامية وغيرهم ) ذلك الدليل لما قام في الأعراض ودل على امتناع بقائها ( طرده النظام في الأجسام فقال بعدم بقائها أيضاً ) . قال الآمدي : وذلك لأنه بني على أصله أن الجواهر مركبة من الأعراض حتى إن كانت الأعراض مختلفة كانت الأجسام . قال : ولهذا فإنا ندرك الاختلاف في بعض الجواهر كالماء والنار بالضرورة كما ندرك الاختلاف بين الحرارة والبرودة كذلك ) ولما كان بقاؤها ضرورياً) أولياً ) التزم الكرامية أنها لا تفنى أصلا بناء على اعتقادهم صحة ذلك الدليل. (وفرق قوم فقالوا : بتجدد الأعراض وبقاء الأجسام وإنما فرقوا بينهما ( بأن الأعراض ) على تقدير فنائها بعدم الشرط بعد بقائها ( مشروطة بالجواهر المشروطة بها فيدور). وتلخيصه أن عدمها بعد بقائها لا يجوز أن يكون بعدم الشرط لأن شرط بقائها لا يجوز أن يكون عرضاً لامتناع التسلسل بل لا يكون ذلك الشرط إلا الجوهر مع كونه مشروطاً بالأعراض في البقاء فيلزم الدور فبطل هذا القسم في الأعراض كسائر الأقسام فثبت أنها لو بقيت لامتنع عدمها لكنها جائزة العدم بالضرورة فلا تكون باقية ( وأما الجواهر فيحفظها الله تعالى بأعراض متعاقبة يخلقها فيها فإذا أراد الله أن يفني) الأجسام (لم يخلق فيها العرض) فتنتفي بانتفاء شرط بقائها ولا محذور فيه، وهذا مذهب الأشاعرة، ( أو يخلق فيها عرضاً منافياً للبقاء ) وهو الفناء مثلاً فينتفي بذلك، وهذا مذهب المعتزلة فلا يتم في الأجسام الدليل الدال على امتناع الفناء بعد البقاء فلا يلزم كونها غير باقية .
Al-Nazzam's argument: If they (bodies) were permanent, their non-existence would be impossible by the proof we mentioned for the permanence of accidents—i.e., regarding the impossibility of their non-existence assuming their permanence—and the consequent is invalid by agreement. A note on the origin of the views of al-Nazzam, the Karamiyya, and others: That proof, when it was established for accidents and indicated the impossibility of their permanence, al-Nazzam applied it analogically to bodies and thus said they are also not permanent. Al-Amidi said: That is because he based it on his principle that substances are composed of accidents, such that if accidents differ, bodies differ. He said: For this reason, we perceive the difference in some substances like water and fire necessarily, just as we perceive the difference between heat and cold. And since their (bodies') permanence is necessary (primary), the Karamiyya committed to the view that they never perish, based on their belief in the soundness of that proof. And some people distinguished, saying: accidents are renewed and bodies are permanent. They only distinguished between them (as follows): accidents, on the assumption of their perishing by absence of condition after their permanence, are conditioned by substances which are themselves conditioned by accidents, leading to circularity. Its summary: Their non-existence after permanence cannot be due to absence of condition, because the condition of their permanence cannot be an accident due to the impossibility of infinite regress; rather, that condition can only be the substance, despite it being conditioned by accidents in permanence, so circularity follows. Thus this category is invalid for accidents like other categories, so it is established that if they were permanent, their non-existence would be impossible, yet they are possibly non-existent by necessity, so they are not permanent. As for substances, God Almighty preserves them by successive accidents that He creates in them. So if God wills to annihilate bodies, He does not create the accident in them, so they cease due to the absence of their condition of permanence, and there is no problem in that; this is the Ash'ari doctrine. Or He creates an accident that is incompatible with permanence, such as annihilation, so they cease by that; this is the Mu'tazili doctrine. Thus the proof indicating the impossibility of perishing after permanence does not hold for bodies, so it does not follow that they are not permanent.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Nazzam's argument against bodily permanence. Al-Nazzam argued that if bodies were permanent, their annihilation would be impossible (by the same proof used for accidents), but annihilation is possible, so bodies are not permanent. The author notes that the Karamiyya accepted the proof for bodies as well, leading them to deny annihilation entirely. The solution is to distinguish between accidents and substances. For accidents, their permanence would entail impossibility of annihilation because any cause of annihilation would lead to circularity or infinite regress. But for bodies (substances), God sustains them by creating successive accidents. He can annihilate them by either ceasing to create the sustaining accident (Ash'ari view) or by creating a destructive accident (Mu'tazili view). Thus the proof does not apply to bodies, and their permanence is affirmed.

Key Terms
حجة النظام
الأعراض
الجواهر
الكَرامية
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nazzam's argument is sound for bodies because accidents and bodies share the same conditions for permanence.
Correction: The passage shows that while accident permanence leads to circularity, body permanence does not, because God sustains bodies through successive accidents.

Checkpoint
What did al-Nazzam apply to bodies to argue they are not permanent?
A) The proof of accident non-permanence
B) The principle of composition
C) The Karamiyya view
D) The circularity argument

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's argument against bodily permanence fails because the conditions for accident permanence lead to circularity, but this does not apply to bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the intricate logical arguments about the permanence of accidents and bodies, do you rely more on rational proofs or on the simple truth that everything except Allah is contingent? Which foundation is firmer in your heart?
?
This passage shows that even great scholars can diverge dramatically—some concluding bodies never perish, others that they do. How does this intellectual humility affect your own confidence in matters of creed? Are you willing to hold your beliefs with both conviction and openness?
?
The refutation of the distinction via circularity reveals that attempts to separate accidents and bodies can lead to logical dead ends. In your own life, where might you be caught in circular reasoning about your faith or actions? What would it take to break that cycle and stand on a clear foundation?

The Ash'ari and Mu'tazili Solutions for Bodily Permanence
Al-Iji presents the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili solutions for bodily permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili schools explain the permanence of bodies and their annihilation.

Core Idea
Bodies are preserved by God through successive accidents; annihilation occurs by removal of that accident or by a contradictory accident.

After establishing that accidents cannot be permanent, the text turns to bodies. This lesson explores the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili solutions, showing how God sustains bodies and how He can annihilate them without logical problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the solution
Read the Arabic and translation from 'As for substances' to the end. Notice the two methods of annihilation.
The Ash'ari view uses cessation of creation; the Mu'tazili uses a destructive accident.
2
Compare the two views
Identify the key difference: Ash'aris annihilate by not creating the sustaining accident, Mu'tazilis by creating a contradictory accident.
Both avoid the circularity that plagued accident permanence.
3
Reflect on the significance
Understand that this refutes al-Nazzam's argument for bodies. The proof for accidents does not apply because bodies have a different condition for permanence (God's creation of sustaining accidents).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the Ash'ari solution, how does God annihilate bodies?
A) By creating a contradictory accident
B) By not creating the accident that sustains them

?

?

?
God could cease sustaining you at any moment. Since you have no control over that, what is the one thing you will do today to show gratitude for His ongoing sustenance?

The Proof from Parallelism Against Infinite Dimensions
Iji summarizes the parallel lines proof that dimensions cannot be infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Proof from Parallelism uses geometric reasoning to show that dimensions cannot be infinite.

Core Idea
If dimensions were infinite, a finite line moving from parallel to concurrent would create an angle with no first point of meeting — a logical impossibility.

This passage presents an argument known as the Proof from Parallelism, which aims to refute the possibility of infinite dimensions. By assuming a finite line parallel to an infinite one and then rotating it until it intersects, the author shows that a contradiction arises: either meeting is impossible or there must be a first meeting point, both of which are absurd. This lesson will guide you through the geometric reasoning and the logical summary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the geometric setup
Read the first part of the passage where the finite line is assumed parallel to the infinite line, then moved to become concurrent. Visualize the configuration described.
The fixed endpoint of the finite line is the center of rotation.
2
Follow the angle divisibility argument
Study how the motion creates an angle at the fixed endpoint. Note the reference to Euclid's theorem that every angle of straight lines can be bisected indefinitely.
This shows that the angle is potentially infinitely divisible.
3
Grasp the paradox of the first point
Understand why there can be no first point of meeting: the angle's infinite divisibility implies that before any supposed first point, a smaller angle (and thus an earlier meeting) exists.
This refutes the notion of a first meeting point.
4
Review the logical summary
Read the author's summary: if infinite dimensions existed, the assumption leads to either impossibility of meeting or existence of a first point, both impossible. Conclude that infinite dimensions are impossible.
The proof is also presented in an alternative form (starting from concurrent then moving to parallel).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السابع: الأبعاد غير متناه [؟] والمسامتة مع ما قبلها أي فوقها من جانب لا تناهي الخط قبل المسامتة معها . وذلك لأن المسامتة مع أية نقطة تفرض إنما تحصل بزاوية مستقيمة الخطين عند الطرف الثابت من الخط المتناهي فأحد الخطين هو هذا المتناهي مفروضاً على وضع الموازاة، والآخر هو بعينه أيضاً لكن حال كونه على المسامتة فكأن هناك خطاً آخر كان منطبقاً عليه فزال بحركته انطباقه مع بقاء أحد طرفيه على حاله، ويزداد اتضاحه بأن نفرض الخط المتناهي خارجاً من مركز كرة موازياً لغير المتناهي ثم نفرض حركتها حتى يصير مسامتاً فيحدث عند مركز الكرة زاوية مستقيمة الخطين وأنها تقبل القسمة إلى غير النهاية إذ قد بين إقليدس في الشكل التاسع من المقالة الأولى من كتابه أن كل زاوية مستقيمة الخطين يمكن تنصيفها بخط مستقيم ولا شك أن كل واحد من النصفين زاوية مستقيمة الخطين فيقبل التنصيف أيضاً، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له على أن الزاوية المسطحة إما كم أو كيفية حالة فيه سارية في جهة واحدة منه فتكون قابلة للانقسام أبداً كالمقادير. وكلما كانت الزاوية أصغر كانت المسامتة مع النقطة الفوقانية يعني إذا فرض أن نقطة ما هي أول نقط المسامتة لم تكن تلك النقطة كذلك لأن المسامتة معها إنما تكون بحدوث زاوية منقسمة إلى نصفين ولا شك أن حدوث نصفها قبل حدوث كلها وفي حال حدوث النصف توجد المسامتة لزوال الموازاة حينئذ قطعا وتلك المسامتة مع نقطة فوقانية بلا شبهة فلا تكون النقطة الأولى في أول نقطة المسامتة وهكذا فلا يمكن أن يوجد هناك ما هو أول تلك النقط وقد تبين ذلك بأن المسامتة إنما تكون بالحركة وكل حركة منقسمة إلى جزء سابق وجزء لاحق فحال ما يوجد الجزء السابق تكون المسامتة مع نقطة أخرى وهكذا قال المصنف : تلخيصه أي تلخيص هذا الوجه أنه لو وجد بعد غير متناه لأمكن الفرض أي المفروض المذكور واللازم باطل لأنه مستلزم إما لامتناع المسامتة أو لوجود نقطة هي أول نقط المسامتة إذ مع ذلك الفرض إما أن تمتنع المسامتة وهو أحد الأمرين أولا تمتنع فيجب أن يوجد أول نقط المسامتة وهو الأمر الآخر والقسمان باطلان أما وجود تلك النقطة فلما استحالته واستلزام وجودها تناهي ما لا يتناهى أيضاً مر من وأما امتناع المسامتة فلان زوال الموازاة بالحركة يستلزم وجودها فلا يتصور امتناعهاء على ذلك الفرض كما لا يخفى، ومنهم من فرض الخط المتناهي أولاً مسامتاً ثم تحرك إلى أن صار موازياً، قال : فلا بد من نقطة هي آخر نقط المسامتة لأنها كانت ثم زالت فيكون لها نهاية لكنه باطل لمثل ما مر وسماه برهان الموازاة .
The second observation - the seventh topic: dimensions are infinite [?] and the meeting with what is before it, i.e., above it from the side of the infinite line, before the meeting with it. And that is because the meeting with any point assumed occurs only by an angle of two straight lines at the fixed endpoint of the finite line. So one of the two lines is this finite line assumed in the position of parallelism, and the other is the same line but in the state of being concurrent. So it is as if there were another line that was coincident with it, then its coincidence ceased by its motion while one of its endpoints remained as it was. And it becomes clearer by assuming the finite line extending from the center of a sphere parallel to the infinite line, then assuming its motion until it becomes concurrent, so at the center of the sphere there occurs an angle of two straight lines, and that it is divisible indefinitely since Euclid showed in the ninth figure of the first article of his book that every angle of two straight lines can be bisected by a straight line, and there is no doubt that each of the two halves is an angle of two straight lines, so it can also be bisected, and so on infinitely. And as for the plane angle, it is either a quantity or a quality subsisting in it flowing in one direction from it, so it is always divisible like magnitudes. And whenever the angle is smaller, the meeting with the upper point is [i.e.,] if it is assumed that a certain point is the first point of meeting, that point would not be such, because the meeting with it only occurs by the occurrence of an angle divisible into two halves, and there is no doubt that the occurrence of its half is before the occurrence of its whole, and in the state of the occurrence of the half, the meeting occurs due to the cessation of parallelism then undoubtedly, and that meeting is with an upper point without doubt, so the first point is not the first point of meeting, and so on. So it is impossible for there to be a first among those points. And that has been made clear by the fact that meeting occurs only by motion, and every motion is divided into a prior part and a posterior part, so when the prior part occurs, the meeting occurs with another point, and so on. The author said: 'Its summary' i.e., the summary of this aspect: that if there were an infinite dimension, then the assumption would be possible, i.e., the aforementioned assumption, and the consequent is false because it necessitates either the impossibility of meeting or the existence of a point that is the first point of meeting. For with that assumption, either meeting is impossible—this is one of the two things—or it is not impossible, so it is necessary that there exists a first point of meeting—the other thing. And both divisions are false. As for the existence of that point, due to its impossibility and its necessitating the finitude of the infinite, also mentioned earlier. And as for the impossibility of meeting, because the cessation of parallelism by motion necessitates its existence, so its impossibility under that assumption is inconceivable, as is not hidden. And among them is one who assumed the finite line first concurrent, then it moved until it became parallel. He said: 'Then there must be a point that is the last point of meeting, because it existed then ceased, so it has an end.' But it is false for the same reason as before. And he called it the proof of parallelism.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a proof known as the 'Proof of Parallelism' (برهان الموازاة) to demonstrate that dimensions cannot be infinite. It begins by defining the geometric situation: a finite line is assumed parallel to an infinite line, then rotated until it becomes concurrent (intersecting) with the infinite line. The key idea is that when the finite line moves from parallel to concurrent, it traces an angle that is always divisible, so there can be no first point of meeting. The author (al-Iji) summarizes the argument: if there were an infinite dimension, we could assume this configuration, but it leads to a dilemma—either meeting is impossible, or there is a first meeting point. Both are impossible: the first because meeting actually occurs (the motion causes it), the second because any supposed first point of meeting would be preceded by another due to the infinite divisibility of the angle. Thus, the assumption of infinite dimensions is false. The proof is attributed to Euclid's geometry.

Key Terms
بعد
مسامتة
موازاة
برهان الموازاة
زاوية مستقيمة الخطين
لا تناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on actual infinite divisibility of space/time, which is not accepted.
Correction: The proof uses potential infinite divisibility (every angle can be bisected), which is a standard mathematical property. It does not require an actual infinite number of divisions.

Checkpoint
What two impossible consequences does the Proof of Parallelism derive from assuming infinite dimensions?
A) Meeting is either impossible or has a first point.
B) The infinite line becomes finite or the angle is not divisible.
C) The sphere collapses or the parallel lines remain parallel.
D) The finite line becomes infinite or the angle becomes a straight line.

Key Takeaway
The Proof from Parallelism shows that assuming infinite dimensions leads to a logical contradiction involving an infinitely divisible angle and the impossibility of a first meeting point, thereby proving that dimensions are finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that an infinite dimension leads to an impossible first point of meeting. In your own spiritual journey, have you been postponing the 'first point' of turning sincerely to God? What specific step will you take today to begin that meeting?
?
If a simple geometric argument can demonstrate that the universe must be finite and dependent on a Creator, what does that say about the excuses you make for not seeking God with full seriousness? Where in your life are you still acting as if this world is infinite and self-sufficient?

Eliminating Natural Movement as the Cause of Celestial Motion
Iji proves celestial movements are not natural via a paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circular celestial motion cannot be natural, and how this leads to the need for voluntary movement.

Core Idea
Circular motion's paradox—seeking and abandoning the same position—undoes natural movement as an explanation for celestial motion.

This section from Iji's Aqaid al-Adudiyya argues that celestial movements are voluntary by first eliminating natural movement. The author uses a clever paradox: in circular motion, every position is both sought and abandoned, which is impossible for a natural motion. We'll explore the argument and its refinement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification of movement
The passage begins by dividing all self-movement into three types: natural, compulsory, and voluntary. This sets up the logical structure of the proof.
This is a common kalam technique: exhaustive division and elimination.
2
Grasp the paradox of circular motion
The author argues that if circular motion were natural, then the same position would be both naturally sought and naturally abandoned—a contradiction. Reflect on why this is impossible.
This is the core of the argument against natural movement.
3
Consider the refinement and reply
The passage includes a response that the abandonment is not of the same position but its like, and then offers a better formulation: the alternating seeking and abandoning requires will and perception.
This strengthens the argument by connecting it to volition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : في النفوس الفلكية وهي مجردة عن المادة وتوابعها ( لأن حركات الأفلاك ) إرادية فلها نفوس مجردة . أما الأول ) وهو كون حركاتها إرادية ( فلأنها إما طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية ) لما مر من أن أقسام الحركة الذاتية منحصرة فيها ( والأولان باطلان ) فتعين الثالث ( أما كونها طبيعية فلأن الحركة الدورية كل ما وضع فيها فهو مطلوب ومتروك فلو كان ذلك التحرك الدوري (مقتضى (الطبيعة) ومستنداً إليها (لكان الشيء الواحد ) وهو الوضع المخصوص ) مطلوباً بالطبع ومتروكاً بالطبع وإنه محال) وقد وجه هذا الدليل بأن كل وضع يتوجه إليه المتحرك بالاستدارة يكون ترك ذلك الوضع هو عين التوجه إليه، فيكون المهروب عنه بالطبع بعينه مطلوباً بالطبع في حالة واحدة بل يكون الهرب عن الشيء عين طلبه وأنه محال بديهة. ورد عليه بأنه ترك وضع ليس توجهاً إليه بعينه لانعدامه بتركه بل غايته أنه توجه إلى مثله فلا يكون المتروك نفس المطلوب فالأولى أن يوجه بأن المتحرك بحركته المستديرة يطلب وضعاً ثم يتركه ومثله لا يتصور من فاقد الإرادة لأن طلب الشيء المعين وتركه لا يكون إلا باختلاف الأغراض الموقوفة على الشعور والإرادة ) وأما كونها قسرية فلما تقدم أن القسر إنما يكون على خلاف الطبع وذلك لأنه تقدم في مباحث الاعتمادات ) هو بمعناه أعني ) أن عديم الميل الطبيعي لا يتحرك قسرا ( وهاهنا لا طبع فلا قسر، وأيضاً فلو كان تحرك الأفلاك على الاستدارة ) بالقسر لكان على موافقة القاسر فوجب تشابه (حركاتها في الجهة والسرعة والبطء وتوافقها في المناطق والأقطاب إذ لا يتصور هناك قسر إلا من بعضها لبعض لكن حركاتها كما شهدت به الأرصاد ليست متشابهة ولا متوافقة .
The first maqsad: On celestial souls, and they are immaterial from matter and its dependents, because the movements of the celestial spheres are voluntary, so they have immaterial souls. As for the first, that their movements are voluntary, then because they are either natural, compulsory, or voluntary, since the divisions of self-movement are limited to these, and the first two are false, so the third is determined. As for them being natural: because in circular movement, every position it enters is both sought and abandoned; if that circular movement were necessitated by nature and based on it, then one thing, namely the particular position, would be both naturally desired and naturally abandoned, and that is impossible. This proof has been expressed as: every position that the mover approaches by rotation, its abandonment is the very same approach, so that which is naturally fled from is precisely that which is naturally desired in one state; rather, the flight from the thing would be the very seeking of it, and that is obviously impossible. It was replied that the abandonment of a position is not exactly an approach to it because it ceases to exist by its abandonment; rather, its utmost is an approach to the like of it, so the abandoned is not the very same as the sought. The better formulation is: the mover in its circular motion seeks a position then abandons it, and the like of this is not conceivable from one lacking will, because seeking a specific thing and abandoning it occurs only due to differing purposes that depend on perception and will. As for them being compulsory: because it was established earlier that compulsion only occurs contrary to nature, and that is because it was established in the discussions of inclinations that one lacking natural inclination does not move by compulsion, and here there is no nature, so no compulsion. Also, if the celestial spheres moved in a circle by compulsion, it would be in accordance with the compressor, so their movements would necessarily be similar in direction, speed, and slowness, and agree in their zones and poles, since compulsion can only be conceived from some of them to others. But their movements, as observed by astronomy, are not similar nor concordant.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a proof that celestial movements are voluntary, the first step in arguing for immaterial celestial souls. The author first classifies all self-movement into three types: natural, compulsory, and voluntary. He then eliminates the first two. The argument against natural movement uses the peculiar feature of circular motion: every position in a circular orbit is both approached and abandoned. If this were natural, the same object would naturally both seek and flee the same position, which is paradoxical. He mentions a refinement of this argument and a response to it, but concludes that the proper formulation is that such alternating seeking and abandoning cannot occur without will (irada) and perception (shu'ur). Against compulsory movement, he gives two arguments: first, compulsion requires a natural inclination to be overcome, but celestial spheres have no natural inclination (since they have no nature in the sense of having a natural place). Second, if they were moved by compulsion from each other, their motions would be uniform in direction, speed, etc., but observations show they are diverse. Therefore, celestial movements must be voluntary. This proof is a classic example of kalam reasoning, using logical division and empirical observation.

Key Terms
حركة طبيعية
حركة قسرية
حركة إرادية
شعور
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think celestial spheres could have a natural circular motion because it is uniform and perpetual.
Correction: The author shows that natural motion cannot account for the simultaneous seeking and abandoning of positions in a circle; this requires voluntary agency.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against celestial movements being natural?
A) They are too fast to be natural.
B) Circular motion involves seeking and abandoning the same position, which is impossible for a natural mover.
C) Natural movement only applies to terrestrial objects.
D) Celestial spheres have souls, so they cannot be natural.

Key Takeaway
Circular motion's inherent alternation between seeking and abandoning positions cannot be natural; it requires a will with perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own movement—your actions—are they driven by nature (habit, impulse) or by conscious will and perception? When you find yourself seeking something and then abandoning it repeatedly (like a resolution you never keep), are you acting as a celestial sphere without will? Examine one specific pattern in your life where you oscillate between seeking and abandoning. What would it take to make that movement truly voluntary and purposeful?
?
The passage argues that alternating seeking and abandoning of the same thing cannot be natural; it requires perception and will. In your prayers or acts of worship, do you sometimes treat them as natural cycles—going through motions without real intention? How can you inject real shu'ur and irada into your daily spiritual routines?
?
If celestial spheres have souls that choose their motion with purpose, what does that say about the purposefulness your own actions should have? Are you content to be moved by external forces (compulsion) or your own lower nature (natural inclination) rather than by a clear, chosen purpose? What is one area where you need to shift from passive movement to active, volitional action?

Eliminating Compulsory Movement and Concluding Voluntariness
Iji eliminates compulsory movement with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why celestial movements cannot be compulsory and how this, combined with the previous argument, proves they are voluntary.

Core Idea
Compulsion requires a nature to overcome, and universal compulsion would produce uniform motions, contradicting observed diversity.

Having eliminated natural movement, the author now turns to compulsory movement. He provides two arguments: first, compulsion presupposes a natural inclination, which celestial spheres lack; second, if they were compelled by each other, their motions would be uniform, but astronomical observations show diversity. Thus, only voluntary movement remains.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first argument against compulsion
The author states that compulsion only occurs against a natural inclination, and since celestial spheres have no nature (being beyond generation and corruption), they cannot be moved by compulsion.
This relies on the earlier discussion of inclinations (اعتمادات).
2
Learn the second argument from diversity of motion
If celestial spheres were compressing each other, their motions would be similar in direction, speed, and zones, but astronomical observations show they are diverse, so compulsion is false.
This is an empirical argument using observed data.
3
Conclude the proof
Since both natural and compulsory movements are eliminated, the remaining possibility is voluntary movement. This necessitates celestial souls with perception and will.
This completes the proof for immaterial celestial souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : في النفوس الفلكية وهي مجردة عن المادة وتوابعها ( لأن حركات الأفلاك ) إرادية فلها نفوس مجردة . أما الأول ) وهو كون حركاتها إرادية ( فلأنها إما طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية ) لما مر من أن أقسام الحركة الذاتية منحصرة فيها ( والأولان باطلان ) فتعين الثالث ( أما كونها طبيعية فلأن الحركة الدورية كل ما وضع فيها فهو مطلوب ومتروك فلو كان ذلك التحرك الدوري (مقتضى (الطبيعة) ومستنداً إليها (لكان الشيء الواحد ) وهو الوضع المخصوص ) مطلوباً بالطبع ومتروكاً بالطبع وإنه محال) وقد وجه هذا الدليل بأن كل وضع يتوجه إليه المتحرك بالاستدارة يكون ترك ذلك الوضع هو عين التوجه إليه، فيكون المهروب عنه بالطبع بعينه مطلوباً بالطبع في حالة واحدة بل يكون الهرب عن الشيء عين طلبه وأنه محال بديهة. ورد عليه بأنه ترك وضع ليس توجهاً إليه بعينه لانعدامه بتركه بل غايته أنه توجه إلى مثله فلا يكون المتروك نفس المطلوب فالأولى أن يوجه بأن المتحرك بحركته المستديرة يطلب وضعاً ثم يتركه ومثله لا يتصور من فاقد الإرادة لأن طلب الشيء المعين وتركه لا يكون إلا باختلاف الأغراض الموقوفة على الشعور والإرادة ) وأما كونها قسرية فلما تقدم أن القسر إنما يكون على خلاف الطبع وذلك لأنه تقدم في مباحث الاعتمادات ) هو بمعناه أعني ) أن عديم الميل الطبيعي لا يتحرك قسرا ( وهاهنا لا طبع فلا قسر، وأيضاً فلو كان تحرك الأفلاك على الاستدارة ) بالقسر لكان على موافقة القاسر فوجب تشابه (حركاتها في الجهة والسرعة والبطء وتوافقها في المناطق والأقطاب إذ لا يتصور هناك قسر إلا من بعضها لبعض لكن حركاتها كما شهدت به الأرصاد ليست متشابهة ولا متوافقة .
The first maqsad: On celestial souls, and they are immaterial from matter and its dependents, because the movements of the celestial spheres are voluntary, so they have immaterial souls. As for the first, that their movements are voluntary, then because they are either natural, compulsory, or voluntary, since the divisions of self-movement are limited to these, and the first two are false, so the third is determined. As for them being natural: because in circular movement, every position it enters is both sought and abandoned; if that circular movement were necessitated by nature and based on it, then one thing, namely the particular position, would be both naturally desired and naturally abandoned, and that is impossible.
